Bibliography 649 Abstract 

After giving a number of properties of continued fractions of polynomial type, in particular focusing on convergence properties and Bauer‚ÄďMuir‚ÄďAp¬ī ery accelera-tion techniques, we give a large list of continued fractions, both for specific real numbers, and for special functions, some extracted from a number of different sources, but most others being probably new. In addition to providing such a list, one of our main additions is to include the exact speed of convergence of these con-tinued fractions (sometimes only up to a multiplicative constant), which is almost always omitted in the literature.   

> 2020 Mathematics Subject Classification. Primary .
> ix

# Preface 

The theory of continued fractions is one of the oldest in mathematics, since implicitly the Greeks already knew the simple continued fraction of the Golden Ratio Ōē = (1 + ‚ąö5) /2 = 1 + 1 /(1 + 1 /(1 + 1 /(1 + ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ ))). Countless books are devoted to the subject (I have included a few in the bibliography, but many more exist) which treat different aspects of the subject: convergence problems, explicit continued fractions for interesting constants and functions, applications in other fields and for solving certain kinds of problems, combinatorial aspects, number-theoretic aspects in particular linked to Diophantine approximation, algorithmic uses, for instance in the numerical computation of inverse Mellin transforms, etc... Nevertheless, the need for the present book arose for several reasons. (1) First, although convergence theorems often form a large and difficult part of the literature, the practical applications of the corresponding results are rarely given. For instance, in some books giving lists of continued fractions, the speed of convergence is mentioned in a non-mathematical way, either using vague words like ‚Äúslowly‚ÄĚ or ‚Äúrapidly‚ÄĚ convergent, or by giving tables showing numerically how suitable convergents approximate the limit. (2) Second, although some books such as [ 20 ] do contain a large number of continued fractions, they are far from complete. Inasmuch as possible, I have tried to collect everything that I could from the abundant literature, and I have also added a large number which seem to be new. In addition, as mentioned, I give a precise estimate of their speed of convergence. (3) Third, it has apparently not been realized until now that the special method used by R. Ap¬ī ery to prove the irrationality of ő∂(3) is in fact applicable to a large number of continued fractions existing in the liter-ature, including continued fractions for functions , thus providing a large number of completely new ones with faster convergence properties. In many cases the corresponding explicit formulas are rather unwieldly, so are not given, but are included when they are reasonably simple. (4) Finally, since this book contains more than 1600 continued fractions, most of them new, it would be wasteful for the reader to have to painstakingly copy each of the ones that interests him/her. We thus also provide a database of all the CFs and relevant information in a file readable by 

Pari/GP , but also by any other software with a suitable interface, since the details of the contents and format of this file are described in Appendix D. The work described in this book would not have been possible without a spe-cialized package for working with continued fractions, which will be described in  

> xi xii PREFACE

detail in Chapter 3. This is part of the Pari/GP computer algebra system, and for now (2024) is only available in the GIT branches henri-CF and henri-ellCF2 

(same, with in addition an implementation of Jacobi elliptic functions), but will hopefully at some point be available in the main distribution. The author would evidently appreciate any corrections and additions which would be included in future editions and on the author‚Äôs website. Note that since most of the given continued fractions are new, and almost all the additional data (exact speed of convergence, asymptotic expansion) do not exist at all in the liter-ature, it is unavoidable that errors (typos or mathematical mistakes) have cropped up, which renders user feedback absolutely essential. Ideally one should put all this data on a web site which could be regularly updated by users, but I do not have the knowledge nor the time to do this. Henri Cohen Brief Personal Recollections 

This book is dedicated to my friends and colleagues Michel Mend` es-France and Alf van der Poorten, who passed away in 2018 and 2010 respectively. I came to settle in Bordeaux in part thanks to the friendly advice of Michel, and my first serious mathematical paper (my very first one was more recreational) was directly inspired by his work on continued fractions: he had studied how the length of a finite CF behaves when multiplied by an integer. I studied the corre-sponding problem for the period of a periodic CF, and for instance proved that, when multiplied by 2, the length of the period is at most multiplied by 5, and 5 is best possible, see [ 8]. Alf came regularly to Bordeaux, in particular at the invitation of Michel, and we had numerous mathematical discussions, in particular related to continued frac-tions. For instance, I remember telling him that I was surprised that no textbook on CFs mentions a necessary and sufficient condition for an irreducible fraction 

p/q to be a partial quotient of the CF of some irrational number őĪ. These texts all mention that a necessary condition is that |őĪ ‚ąí p/q | ‚Č§ 1/q 2, and that a sufficient 

(but certainly not necessary) condition is that |őĪ ‚ąí p/q | ‚Č§ 1/(2 q2). But in fact a necessary and sufficient condition is that ‚ąí1/(q(q + p‚Ä≤) < őĪ ‚ąí p/q < 1/(q(2 q ‚ąí p‚Ä≤)), where p‚Ä≤ is the inverse of p modulo q such that 1 ‚Č§ p‚Ä≤ ‚Č§ q, see for instance Exercise 21 of Chapter 5 of [ 9]. Among the many things that Alf taught me was the marvelous subject of ‚Äúunlikely intersections‚ÄĚ, related simultaneously to torsion points on Jacobians of curves, to the periodicity of continued fractions of square roots of polynomials, to Somos sequences, and to the integrability in elementary terms of hyperelliptic integrals, see for instance Chapter 5 of [ 6]. Henri Cohen

# Basic Results on Continued Fractions 

1.1. Notation 

Although we assume the reader familiar with the usual terminology and basic results on continued fractions, we prefer starting from scratch and in particular explain our specific notation. First, note that to avoid always writing ‚Äúcontinued fraction‚ÄĚ, we will often use the abbreviation ‚ÄúCF‚ÄĚ. Let ( a(n)) n‚Č•0 and ( b(n)) n‚Č•0 be two (finite or infinite) sequences of complex numbers. The continued fraction ( a, b ) is the formal expression (a, b ) = a(0) + b(0) 

a(1) + b(1)

a(3) + . . .which we can write more compactly as ( a, b ) = a(0)+ b(0) /(a(1)+ b(1) /(a(2)+ ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ )). Note that this notation is different from that used by other authors such as [ 20 ]. I do not claim that my notation is better but simply that it is the one used in the present book and by the continued fraction package of Pari/GP (currently the GIT 

branches origin/henri-CF and origin/henri-ellCF2 ). If a(n) and b(n) are finite sequences, this is simply a rational function in the coefficients, so not particularly interesting. Similarly if b(n) = 0 for some n. We will therefore always assume that the sequences are infinite and that b(n)Őł = 0 for all n. Although not strictly necessary we also assume that a(n) = 0 only for a finite 

number of n.We write as usual formally

We say that the continued fraction converges to a limit S if S = lim n‚Üí‚ąě p(n)/q (n)exists. To estimate the speed of convergence means to give an estimate for S ‚ąí

p(n)/q (n). As a matter of terminology, a(n) and b(n) are called the numerators and de-nominators of the CF respectively, or simply its coefficients. ( p(n), q (n)) are called the partial quotients , and p(n)/q (n) the convergents .We will use the following practical notation: 

Definition 1.1 . For any arithmetic function f (n) we define f !( n) = Q 

> 1‚Č§j‚Č§n

f (j), so in particular f !(0) = 1. Note that we do not include the value f (0), even if it is defined, so that for instance Q

Definition 1.2 . We say that two continued fractions ( a(n), b (n)) and ( a‚Ä≤(n), b ‚Ä≤(n)) are equivalent if there exists a nonzero sequence r(n) with r(0) = 1 such that 

a‚Ä≤(n) = r(n)a(n) and b‚Ä≤(n) = r(n)r(n + 1) b(n). The main reason for this notion is the following lemma: 

Lemma 1.3 . If two continued fractions are equivalent they have the same con-vergents p(n)/q (n), and in particular the same limits and convergence speed if they converge. 

Proof. Indeed, using the notation in the definition, it is immediate to check by induction that if ( p(n), q (n)) and ( p‚Ä≤(n), q ‚Ä≤(n)) are the corresponding partial quotients we have ( p‚Ä≤(n), q ‚Ä≤(n)) = r!( n)( p(n), q (n)). ‚Ė°

Remarks 1.4 . (1) In particular note that if a(n) and b(n) are rational functions, by using a suitable r(n) we can find an equivalent continued fraction ( a‚Ä≤(n), b ‚Ä≤(n)) where a‚Ä≤(n) and b‚Ä≤(n) are polynomials. (2) This notion of equivalence is not the same as one which is often used in the case of simple continued fractions, where it means that the CFs have the same tails (i.e., there exists n0 such that a‚Ä≤(n) = a(n + n0) for n

sufficiently large), or almost equivalently that their limits are linked by a M¬® obius transformation z‚Ä≤ = ( Az + B)/(Cz + D). Finally note the important transformation of continued fractions called con-tractions (even or odd): this consists in constructing the natural CF whose partial quotients are ( p(2 n), q (2 n)) (or ( p(2 n + 1) , q (2 n + 1)) for the odd contraction). For instance, the even contraction is obtained by the continued fraction ( A(n), B (n)) with 

A(0) = a(0) , A (1) = a(1) a(2) + b(1)

a(2 n) for n ‚Č• 1 . The presence of denominators in these formulas means that usually the correspond-ing expressions are rational functions, hence more complicated than the original continued fraction. Thus, when given continued fractions of period 2 (see defini-tion below), which are extremely frequent, although it may be tempting to use contractions to obtain CFs of period 1, it is often not a good idea. 1.2. TYPES OF CONTINUED FRACTIONS 3

1.2. Types of Continued Fractions 

The simplest type of continued fraction (and historically the first to be consid-ered) is the simple type: a CF is simple if b(n) = 1 for all n and a(n) is a strictly positive integer for n ‚Č• 1 ( a(0) can be arbitrary). This is the type which is used in most elementary questions of Diophantine approximation because of the following standard properties: (1) The simple CF expansion of a rational number is finite, and conversely. (2) The simple CF expansion of a real quadratic irrational ( z = a + b‚ąöd with 

a, b rational with bŐł = 0 and d a non square positive integer) is periodic and conversely. (3) A simple CF always converges (at an exponential rate). In addition, if 

p(n)/q (n) are the convergents of the CF expansion of an irrational number 

z, we have q(n)p(n ‚ąí 1) ‚ąí p(n)q(n ‚ąí 1) = ( ‚ąí1) n, |z ‚ąí p(n)/q (n)| < 1/q (n)2

(more precise inequalities exist), and in a suitable sense the rational num-bers p(n)/q (n) are the ‚Äúbest‚ÄĚ rational approximations to z.Although ‚Äúsimple‚ÄĚ continued fractions are essential for Diophantine questions, they are only a tiny fraction of the theory, and it is natural to consider the more general CFs as presented above. Before coming to the polynomial type, which is essentially the only type studied in the present book, we give a few samples of other types, of course far from being exhaustive. (1) Simple continued fractions are intimately linked to the Euclidean algo-rithm and the identity a = bq + r with 0 ‚Č§ r < b . But we can also consider negative continued fractions, corresponding to the above iden-tity with ‚ąíb < r ‚Č§ 0: this corresponds to choosing b(n) = ‚ąí1 for all n.The first two properties above are preserved (not the third), but perhaps what is more interesting is the link with deeper theories, for instance in topology and number theory. More generally, we can consider b(n) = e aconstant, and in that case the corresponding CF of a quadratic irrational is not necessarily periodic (for instance e = 3 and z = ‚ąö29; the reader can play the game of finding for each small positive or negative integer e

the list of nonsquare d ‚Č§ 100 such that z = ‚ąöd does not apparently have a periodic CF with b(n) = e). (2) A very common type of CF that we will not consider is what one can call 

q-continued fractions. One of the most famous is the Ramanujan CF with 

a(0) = 0, a(n) = 1 for n ‚Č• 1, b(0) = q1/5, and b(n) = qn for n ‚Č• 1, in other words 

R(q) = q1/5/(1 + q/ (1 + q2/(1 + q3/(1 + ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ )))) .

This has many modular interpretations and infinite product representa-tions, but one of its most striking properties is its CM-properties , in that it takes explicit algebraic values when q = eiŌÄŌĄ with ŌĄ a quadratic irrational with strictly positive imaginary part: for instance R(e‚ąí2ŌÄ ) is a positive root of the polynomial equation x4 + 2 x3 ‚ąí 6x2 ‚ąí 2x + 1 = 0. (3) Still other types of CFs are when one chooses a(n) and/or b(n) to be certain special types of sequences such as automatic sequences .In the present book we will only consider CFs of polynomial type , according to the following definition: 4 1. BASIC RESULTS ON CONTINUED FRACTIONS 

Definition 1.5 . A CF (( a(n)) n‚Č•0, (b(n)) n‚Č•0) is said to be of polynomial type if there exists a period T such that for every j with 0 ‚Č§ j < T , for n sufficiently large both a(nT + j) and b(nT + j) are rational functions of n with rational coefficients. The name polynomial type is justified since as we have mentioned above, any CF with rational function coefficients is equivalent to one with polynomial coefficients, and since we assume rational coefficients, if desired we may also assume that the polynomials have integer coefficients. In practice T will be equal to 1 or 2 (in very exceptional cases 3, 4, or 5), so for 

n sufficiently large either a(n) and b(n) are rational functions, or a(2 n), a(2 n + 1), 

b(2 n), and b(2 n + 1) are rational functions. The only case where T is often large is for continued fractions associated to real quadratic irrationals, which we will not consider. 

1.3. Transformations of Series and Products into CFs 

It is well-known at least since Euler that one can transform a series into a continued fraction in trivial ways, so that the nth partial sum of the series is equal to the nth convergent of the continued fraction. Let us see the different ways in which this is done. 

Definition 1.6 . We will say that a continued fraction is termwise equal to an infinite series (resp., to an infinite product) if its nth convergent p(n)/q (n) is equal to the nth partial sum of the series (resp., to the nth partial product of the infinite product). 

Proposition 1.7 (Euler) . Let f and g be two arithmetic functions, and let S

Because of these (trivial) results, any constant or function which is given by a hypergeometric expression (i.e., the ratio of two consecutive terms is a rational function of m) has automatically a corresponding CF. Since a very large number of constants and functions are given in this way, this provides correspondingly a very large number of CFs. These CFs are not interesting per se (no more and no less than the corresponding hypergeometric series), but one of the main interests lies in the fact that these CFs can be transformed into completely new CFs, using transformations which are not possible (at least not directly) on the series. We will see numerous examples of this. Although less useful in practice, similar results hold for infinite products instead of infinite series: indeed, we have the trivial identity

# Convergence Results 

A large part of the contents of this chapter can also be found in Chapter 7 of [1]. 

2.1. Introduction 

In all books devoted to continued fractions, a large part deals with the difficult problems related to convergence. This can however be considerably simplified if we impose the following restrictions: first, we only consider CFs of polynomial type as defined in the previous chapter. And second, we accept generic and heuristic 

results. Let us give an example to explain these restrictions. The partial quotients (p(n), q (n)) of a CF satisfy a 3-term recursion, for instance q(n + 1) = a(n +1) q(n) + b(n)q(n ‚ąí 1). As we will see below, studying the speed of convergence of a CF is essentially equivalent to finding the asymptotic behavior of q(n). Consider for instance the recursion u(n + 1) = 5 u(n) ‚ąí 6u(n ‚ąí 1). The general solution is 

A3n+1 + B2n+1 for some constants A and B depending on the initial values u(‚ąí1) and u(0), more precisely A = u(0) ‚ąí 2u(‚ąí1) and B = 3 u(‚ąí1) ‚ąí u(0). Thus, if 

u(0) Őł = 2 u(‚ąí1) we have u(n) ‚ąľ C ¬∑ 3n for some nonzero constant C = 3 A, and this is what we call the generic solution. But in the special case u(0) = 2 u(‚ąí1) we have u(n) ‚ąľ C ¬∑ 2n with C = 2 B, which is completely different but special, i.e., nongeneric. We will see the heuristic part in the way the proof of the main result is obtained. 

2.2. The Main Result 

We will first assume that T = 1, so that a(n) and b(n) are rational functions of n for n sufficiently large. We write 

a(n) = a0nőĪ(1 + a1/n + a2/n 2 + ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ ) and b(n) = b0nő≤ (1 + b1/n + b2/n 2 + ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ )with a0b0Őł = 0 (we only use aj and bj in this section, which of course should not be confused with a(j) and b(j)). We will see below that all of our asymptotics (excluding the exceptional case of logarithmic convergence, see below) will be of the form 

u(n) ‚ąľ n!F Ene(Dn )1/2

nP C , 

where C, D, E, and F are real constants with D ‚Č• 0, EŐł = 0 and CŐł = 0, so to avoid a cumbersome notation we will simply write u(n) = [ F, E, D, P ], omitting the constant C. Typically this will be applied to u(n) = q(n) or u(n) = 1 /(S ‚ąí

p(n)/q (n)). The main result is as follows. Although we call it a ‚Äútheorem‚ÄĚ, we recall that it is valid in a generic situation and that the proof is heuristic, although it can

be justified rigorously in many cases. Since it has a large number of cases it is extremely technical but absolutely fundamental.

Remarks 2.2 . (1) In the cases not covered by the above theorem, it is reasonable to expect that the CF does not converge, but this may not be the case. (2) As emphasized above, these convergence types are valid generically, so in very special cases may be incorrect. Additional errors may come from the fact that the proof below is partly heuristic. (3) An important remark also linked to genericity concerns the square roots which must be taken in the following cases: for the exponential type, 

őī = ‚ąö‚ąÜ, for type P ‚ąí we must compute ‚ąöb0, and for type P + we must compute ‚ąöB. In these cases it is understood that we must choose the positive square root, which corresponds to the generic situation. However when the CF depends on one or more variables, we must be careful to take the correct square root, i.e., to add suitable absolute values: ‚ąöz2 = |z|.(4) A trivial remark: do not confuse the notion of polynomial convergence 

type as above, with the notion of CFs of polynomial type, which means that a(n) and b(n) are polynomials for large n. I will try to always include the words ‚Äúconvergence type‚ÄĚ when necessary.

Putting everything together and coming back to the general case of ( a(n), b (n)) proves the result, in a heuristic manner. 

2.4. Additional Remarks 2.4.1. Continued Fractions with Period T > 1. In the case of CFs with period T > 1, the convergence can be given only for each congruence class modulo 

T . Consider by far the most frequent case T = 2. Using the above theorem on the contracted CF we can give asymptotic estimates for S ‚ąí p(2 n)/q (2 n) and 

S ‚ąí p(2 n + 1) /q (2 n + 1) separately. Usually, only the constant will change between the two, so we will only consider S ‚ąí p(2 n)/q (2 n). The following lemma is trivial: 

Lemma 2.6 . Assume that S ‚ąí p(2 n)/q (2 n) = [ F, E, D, P ]. Then for n even we have 

S ‚ąí p(n)/q (n) = [ F/ 2, ‚ąöE/ 2F/ 2, D/ 2, P + F/ 4] .

Thus, when we give the speed of convergence of a period 2 CF, we use the formula given by this lemma. One important thing to note is that one must compute the square root of E:as mentioned above, this means the positive square root, and care must be taken when E depends on one or more variables. But in addition, in rare cases E can be 2.4. ADDITIONAL REMARKS 17 

negative: in that case it is possible that the CF does not converge, but if it does and E = ‚ąí1, we indicate this by P i or Di instead of P ‚ąí and D‚ąí. In more detail: if 

E = 1, for n sufficiently large the signs of successive convergents is (+ , +, +, +, . . . )or ( ‚ąí, ‚ąí, ‚ąí, ‚ąí, . . . ); if E = ‚ąí1 it is (+ , ‚ąí, +, ‚ąí, . . . ) or ( ‚ąí, +, ‚ąí, +, . . . ); if E = i it is (+ , +, ‚ąí, ‚ąí) up to cyclic permutation.

CONVERGENCE RESULTS 

2.4.4. Irregular Continued Fractions. Although we only mention CFs with a given period T (almost always T = 1 or T = 2), it is worth mentioning that there exist natural CFs whose coefficients are completely irregular, but nonetheless ap-parently converge, and in fact sub-exponentially with type D¬Ī (although to the author‚Äôs knowledge, no proof is known): these are CFs coming from the transfor-mation of asymptotic expansions of inverse Mellin transforms. For instance the CF for the inverse Mellin transform of őď( s)3 (which is easy to create explicitly, for instance using the Pari/GP function gammamellininvasymp ) seems to converge subexponentially, but as far as I am aware one does not even know how to prove that it converges at all. I refer to Chapter 8 of [ 1] for a detailed description and discussion of this subject. CHAPTER 3 

# Numerical Evaluations 

3.1. Introduction 

Now that we know the convergence behavior of a continued fraction, by far the most important next step is to numerically compute its limit, and the method used is of course entirely dependent on its speed of convergence. We summarize these methods and then give details in the next section. Write S ‚ąí p(n)/q (n) = 1/[F, E, D, P ]. (1) Convergence type F and E: this is very rapid convergence, so we simply compute a sufficiently large N such that N !F EN > 2B , where B is the desired number of bits (with possibly a small additional correction if P

is negative), and p(N )/q (N ) will be our numerical approximation to the limit. (2) Convergence type D¬Ī: although the convergence is slower and we could try to be clever, we simply compute a sufficiently large N such that 

e(DN )1/2

> 2B , in other words N > (B log(2)) 2/D . If the desired ac-curacy is not too large, this is sufficient. (3) Convergence type P ¬Ī: in this case we have |S ‚ąí p(n)/q (n)| ‚ąľ C/n P , so it is essential to use some kind of extrapolation method, which we will describe in detail in the next section. (4) Logarithmic convergence type L: in this case S ‚ąí p(n)/q (n) ‚ąľ C/ log( n), so it is absolutely impossible to use any kind of extrapolation method, which would require computing p(n)/q (n) for exponentially large values of n. There is, however, a possible solution which often works: we use 

Bauer‚ÄďMuir acceleration (see Chapter 4) to transform our CF into one which converges to the same limit and has convergence type P ¬Ī. When this is possible we win, otherwise we give up. 

3.2. The Polynomial Type: Extrapolation Methods 

In this section we assume that we are in convergence type P ¬Ī, so that we want to extrapolate. Note that Chapter 2 of [ 1] is entirely devoted to extrapolation methods, however since our present situation is simpler, it is not difficult to choose a reasonably simple and efficient method. Indeed, we know that S ‚ąí p(n)/q (n) ‚ąľ őĶnC/n P with őĶ = ¬Ī1, but if we refine the proof given in Chapter 2 it is possible to show that when P is not integral there is an asymptotic expansion 

S ‚ąí p(n)

q(n) = őĶn

nP (C0 + C1/n + C2/n 2 + ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ )for suitable constants Ci with C0 = CŐł = 0.  

> 19 20 3. NUMERICAL EVALUATIONS

When P is integral, we may also have an asymptotic expansion of the above shape, but it may also involve log( n), in which case the extrapolation methods that we use may only give a few correct decimals. In that case, it may be useful to first apply one or two Bauer‚ÄďMuir transformations before extrapolating. Note that in the case of convergence type DőĶ we would have instead 

S ‚ąí p(n)/q (n) = őĶn/e (Dn )1/2

(C0 + C1/2/n 1/2 + C1/n + C3/2/n 3/2 + ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ ) ,

and the asymptotic expansion in powers of 1 /n 1/2 would slightly complicate mat-ters, although it can also be dealt with, see again loc. cit. In the case that we have an asymptotic expansion in powers of 1 /n we can use a simple method based on Lagrange interpolation. The only thing to be taken care of is the fact that the exponent P may not be integral. We extract from [ 1] the following algorithm adapted to our specific needs, where in particular is explained the choice of the magic constants 1 /5, 10, and 4 /3: 

Proposition 3.1 . Let B be some positive integer, and let p(n)/q (n) be the convergents of a continued fraction of polynomial convergence type P ¬Ī, and assume that P is an integer . We choose N = 1 + ‚ĆąB/ 5‚ĆČ, and for n ‚Č§ N we define wN,n =(‚ąí1) N ‚ąín Nn

Of course there are more intelligent ways to compute the above expression than simply as it is written. When P is not an integer, there are similar but more complicated expressions which allow the computation of the limit, see [ 1] once again. Once the limit S obtained, one can use similar extrapolation methods to find the constant C such that S ‚ąí p(n)/q (n) ‚ąľ C/ (n!F Ene‚ąöDn nP ). Let us give an example. Applying Euler‚Äôs transformation Proposition 1.7 to the series ŌÄ/ 4 = 1 ‚ąí 1/3 + 1 /5 ‚ąí 1/7 + ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ we obtain the continued fraction 

ŌÄ = 4 /(1 + 1 /(2 + 9 /(2 + 25 /(2 + 49 /(2 + 81 /(2 + ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ )))))) ,

which is termwise equal to the series hence converges very slowly, like ( ‚ąí1) n/n ,convergence speed which can also be obtained (apart from the constant) from The-orem 2.1. Thus, to obtain 10 decimals, whether from the series, or from the CF, would require 10 10 terms. Using the above method, to obtain 115 decimal digits we work with 155 digits of internal accuracy and use only 78 terms up to n = 780, which shows the amazing efficiency of this method. As an aside, note that one of the fastest ways to evaluate the series (as opposed to the CF) is to use the sumalt 

function of Pari/GP which is a general method for summing regular alternating series. 

3.3. The Pari/GP Continued Fractions Package 

The rest of this chapter can be skipped on first reading especially if you are not familiar with Pari/GP , but the commands explained in this chapter have been 3.3. THE PARI/GP CONTINUED FRACTIONS PACKAGE 21 

essential in the construction of the encyclopedic dictionary which constitutes the rest of this book. For now, the Pari/GP continued fractions package is not part of the main distribution, but can be downloaded using GIT from the Pari/GP branch henri-CF 

(read the explanations for obtaining such branches on the Pari/GP website). For now I will describe the main available basic commands, and refer to later chapters for more advanced commands such as cfbauer and cfapery . Also, I will not give all the possible options but only the most useful ones. 

3.3.1. Basic Formats. Let [ A, B ] = (( a(n)) n‚Č•0, (b(n)) n‚Č•0) be a continued fraction of polynomial type (not necessarily of polynomial convergence type). If A

(similarly B) has period 1, i.e., if a(n) is a polynomial for n ‚Č• n0 for some n0, we will represent A as the vector 

A = [ a(0) , a (1) , . . . , a (n0 ‚ąí 1) , a (n)] ,

where for i ‚Č§ n0 ‚ąí 1 the a(i) do not depend on n, while a(n) is a polynomial (exceptionally also a rational function) in the reserved variable n. Note that in the package the variable n is reserved for this purpose and cannot be used elsewhere. It would perhaps have been more logical to use the variable x instead, but the variable 

n is almost universally used in texts so I have chosen to use it. Note also that the representation is not unique, for instance we also have A =[a(0) , a (1) , . . . , a (n0 ‚ąí 1) , a (n0), a (n)]. For instance, the continued fraction that we have seen above 

ŌÄ = 4 /(1 + 1 /(2 + 9 /(2 + 25 /(2 + 49 /(2 + 81 /(2 + ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ )))))) is represented by 

[[0,1,2],[4,(2*n-1)^2]] 

If A has period 2, i.e., if a(2 n) and a(2 n + 1) are possibly different polynomials for n large, we will represent A as a vector of 2-component vectors 

A = [[ a(0) , a (1)] , . . . , [a(2 m0 ‚ąí 2) , a (2 m0 ‚ąí 1)] , [a(2 n), a (2 n + 1)]] .

For instance, the continued fraction 

ŌÄ = 4 ‚ąí 4/(3 + 9 /(5 + 4 /(7 + 25 /(9 + 16 /(11 + 49 /(13 + 36 /(15 + ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ ))))))) is represented by 

[[4,2*n+1],[[-4,9],[(2*n)^2,(2*n+3)^2]]] 

Note that in this case all the components must be two-component vectors. For instance the above could not be written as 

[[4,2*n+1],[-4,[(2*n+1)^2,(2*n)^2]]] 

This is a restriction of the package which may be lifted in the future. Continued fractions of period strictly larger than 2 are not supported by the package. A number of special output formats (which therefore cannot be used as inputs to other functions) also exist: see in particular below the output of cffromquad 

and cffromser .22 3. NUMERICAL EVALUATIONS 

3.3.2. Creators and Asymptotics. We now list (with examples) the GP 

commands allowing us to create continued fractions, usually infinite, but sometimes also finite. A useful command to visualize continued fractions is cftochar , as we will see below in all the examples. Note: since the variable n plays such a special role, it is useful (although not strictly necessary) to write ‚Äú n=‚Äôn; ‚ÄĚ at the beginning of each GP session. (1) As mentioned above, the most common way is using vectors. Example with period T = 1: 

? ab=[[1,n-z],[z,z*n]];cftochar(ab,5) % = "1+z/(-z+1+z/(-z+2+2*z/(-z+3+3*z/(-z+4+4*z/(-z+5)))))" ? log(cflimit(subst(ab,z,2))) % = 2.0000000000000000000000000000000000000000000000 ? cftype(ab) % = [[1, 1, 0, 0], [1, -1/z, 0, 1], 2] 

The cflimit command computes the limit, using essentially Propo-sition 3.1 above (in the present example the limit is equal to exp( z)). The 

cftype command gives the speed of convergence using Theorem 2.1: the first component says that q(n) ‚ąľ C ¬∑ n! for some nonzero constant C, the second that exp( z) ‚ąí p(n)/q (n) ‚ąľ C‚Ä≤/(n!( ‚ąí1/z )n ¬∑ n) ‚ąľ (‚ąíz)n/(n + 1)!, so that we are in the factorial type F 1, which for now is indicated by type number 2. Note that since equivalent continued fractions can give the same p(n)/q (n), the really important quantity is exp( z) ‚ąí p(n)/q (n), the asymptotics of q(n) itself being important only for arithmetic applications such as irrationality measures. We can even hope to compute the constants C and C‚Ä≤ using the command cfasymp : note that cflimit and cfasymp cannot include un-specified variables such as z, so we must always use subst . For instance, continuing the above example: 

? cfasymp(subst(ab,z,2)) % = [7.38..., [1, 1, 0, 0, 0.13533...], [1, -1/2, 0, 1, 109.1963...], 2] ? cfasymp(subst(ab,z,3)) % = [20.08..., [1, 1, 0, 0, 0.04978...], [1, -1/3, 0, 1, 1210.28...], 2] 

We easily recognize that 0 .13533 ... = exp( ‚ąí2), that 109 .1963 ... = 2 ¬∑

exp(4), that 0 .04978 ... = exp( ‚ąí3), and 1210 .28 ... = 3 ¬∑ exp(6). It is thus quite plausible (and indeed true) that C = exp( ‚ąíz) and C‚Ä≤ = z exp(2 z). Remark on cftype , cflimit , and cfasymp : these three functions play complementary roles. First, cftype is the only one which accepts unspecified variables such as z as above. However, as already mentioned, it may give an incorrect answer since it often needs to compute square roots, whose sign may depend on z, so care should be used in interpreting the answer. Second, cflimit is quite robust, while cfasymp which also gives the convergence constants C and C‚Ä≤ may fail. (2) Example of period T = 2: 

? ab=[[0,1],[[1,1/2],[n,n+1]/(4*n+2)]]; 3.3. THE PARI/GP CONTINUED FRACTIONS PACKAGE 23 

? cftochar(ab) %="1/(1+1/2/(1+1/6/(1+1/3/(1+1/5/(1+3/10/1)))))" ? exp(cflimit(ab)) %=2.0000000000000000000000000000000000000000000 ? cftype(ab) %=[0, 1.207..., 0, 0], [0, -5.828..., 0, 0], 6] ? cfasymp(ab) %=[0.693..., [0, 1.207..., 0, 0, 1.015...], [0, -5.828..., 0, 0, 1.078...], 6] 

We recognize 1 .207 ... = (1 + ‚ąö2) /2 and ‚ąí5.828 ... = ‚ąí(3 + 2 ‚ąö2). The limit is thus log(2), we are in exponential type E, here indicated by the index 6, and q(n) ‚ąľ C ¬∑ ((1 + ‚ąö2) /2) n and log(2) ‚ąí p(n)

q(n) ‚ąľ (‚ąí1) n C‚Ä≤

(3 + 2 ‚ąö2) n = ( ‚ąí1) n C‚Ä≤

(1 + ‚ąö2) 2n ,

and we recognize that C = 1 .015 ... = (1 + ‚ąö2) /25/4 and C‚Ä≤ = 1 .078 ... =2ŌÄ/ (3 + 2 ‚ąö2). Note on constant recognition: this is of course done with the lindep 

command. In most cases the constants C, C‚Ä≤, etc... are obtained as 

products , so we search for linear combinations involving logarithms of the constants involved, with in addition log( ŌÄ) and log( p) for small primes p.A typical command for recognizing the constants in the above example would be 

lindep([-log(C),log(1+sqrt(2)),log(Pi),log(2),log(3)]) 

It is also sometimes useful to add constants such as log(őď(1 /3)) or log(őď(1 /4)). (3) Completely different ways of creating continued fractions in GP are by using the command cffromser(f,g) , which can have several different meanings. The two most important ones are the following: (a) If f and g are both closures, return the continued fraction corre-sponding to Euler‚Äôs transformation of the series P 

> j‚Č•1

g!( j ‚ąí 1) /f !( j)as in 1.7. For instance: 

? ab=cffromser(n->n^2,n->n^2) % = [[0, 2*n^2 - 2*n + 1], [1, -n^4]] ? cftochar(ab) % = "1/(1-1/(5-16/(13-81/(25-256/(41-625/(61))))))" ? cflimit(ab)-Pi^2/6 % = 0.E-38 ? cftype(ab) % = [[2, 1, 0, 0], [0, 1, 0, 1], 8] 

Of course, the speed of convergence has not been improved since the continued fraction is identical to the series. (b) The second important meaning of cffromser is when f is not a closure, but a formal power series, in which case g must be a small nonnegative integer (or omitted, same as 0) considered as the degree of the polynomial a(n). 3.3.3. Other Tools. We have already mentioned the fundamental cflimit 

command which uses generalizations of Proposition 3.1 to give a numerical approxi-mation to the limit of a continued fraction. Note a limitation of the present version: the above proposition is useful when the exponent P of polynomial convergence is integral, and we have mentioned that generalizations exist, but in fact only when 

P is a rational number with denominator d ‚Č§ 4, say. Otherwise the package will try to guess a numerical limit, but expect it to have only a few if any correct digits. We have also mentioned cfasymp which allows the computation of the constants entering in the asymptotics of q(n) and of S ‚ąí p(n)/q (n), and the same limitations apply, even more strongly. We have mentioned cftochar to visualize a CF, but in addition all the CFs of this book have been printed using the function cftotex which gives a correct TEX output and attempts to remove some (but not always all) unnecessary parentheses. Example: 

? A=[[0,(2+z)*(2*n-1)],[2*z,-n^2*z^2]]; ? cftochar(A) % = "2*z/(z+2-z^2/(3*z+6-4*z^2/(5*z+10-9*z^2/ (7*z+14-16*z^2/(9*z+18-25*z^2/(11*z+22))))))" ? print(cftotex(A)) \dfrac{2z}{z+2-\dfrac{z^2}{3z+6-\dfrac{4z^2}{5z+10-\dfrac{9z^2}{7z+14 -\dfrac{16z^2}{9z+18-\dfrac{25z^2}{11z+22-\ddots}}}}}}

# Bauer‚ÄďMuir‚ÄďAp¬ī ery Acceleration 

4.1. Introduction 

Many continued fractions occurring in the literature and in the present book have a polynomial convergence type P ¬Ī as mentioned above. This is for instance the case for continued fractions obtained by trivial transformations of slowly convergent series (using Euler‚Äôs transformation, i.e., the cffromser command). These can be accelerated using the so-called Bauer‚ÄďMuir method (see for instance [ 1] for details), and the crucial contribution of R. Ap¬ī ery is to have noticed that in some cases (for him, the ‚Äútrivial‚ÄĚ CFs for ő∂(2) and ő∂(3) obtained by applying Euler‚Äôs transformation of series) this acceleration method can be iterated without losing the simplicity of the coefficients, and combined with a diagonal process, usually leads to an exponentially convergent continued fraction (type E), in practice almost always with |E| an integral power of (1 + ‚ąö2) 2 or of ((1 + ‚ąö5) /2) 5. This new continued fraction will have period 2 in the above sense, meaning that ( a(2 n), b (2 n)) and (a(2 n + 1) , b (2 n + 1)) are given by different formulas. What is remarkable and may not have been noticed before is that a large proportion of the CFs which can be found in the literature (and in the present dictionary) can be accelerated in this way. In every case that it does and gives rea-sonably simple coefficients we have given the corresponding new continued fraction, which usually does not appear in the literature. In rare cases (such as those used by Ap¬ī ery in his proof of the irrationality of 

ő∂(2) and ő∂(3)), the even (or odd) contraction of this period 2 continued fraction gives a period 1 continued fraction with reasonably simple coefficients. 

4.2. Bauer‚ÄďMuir Acceleration 

To keep this book self-contained, we first explain the classical Bauer‚ÄďMuir acceleration method. Let ( a(n), b (n)) be a convergent continued fraction, let r(n)be (for now) an arbitrary sequence, and set 

d(n) = r(n)( a(n + 1) + r(n + 1)) ‚ąí b(n) .

We will assume that d(n)Őł = 0 for all n. We define a new continued fraction (a‚Ä≤(n), b ‚Ä≤(n)) by setting 

a‚Ä≤(0) = a(0) + r(0) , b‚Ä≤(0) = ‚ąíd(0) , a‚Ä≤(1) = a(1) + r(1) ,a‚Ä≤(n) = a(n) + r(n) ‚ąí r(n ‚ąí 2) d(n ‚ąí 1) /d (n ‚ąí 2) for n ‚Č• 2, and 

b‚Ä≤(n) = b(n ‚ąí 1) d(n)/d (n ‚ąí 1) for n ‚Č• 1 .

A formal computation shows that if ( p‚Ä≤(n), q ‚Ä≤(n)) are the partial quotients of this new continued fraction, we have ( p‚Ä≤(n), q ‚Ä≤(n)) = ( p(n), q (n))+ r(n)( p(n‚ąí1) , q (n‚ąí1)) (this is of course how the formulas for a‚Ä≤(n) and b‚Ä≤(n) were obtained).  

> 25 26 4. BAUER‚ÄďMUIR‚ÄďAP ¬īERY ACCELERATION

Note that if we denote by ŌĀ(n) = b(n)/(a(n+1)+ b(n+1) /(a(n+2)+ ... )) the nth 

tail of the continued fraction, we have trivially ŌĀ(n)( a(n + 1) + ŌĀ(n + 1)) ‚ąí b(n) = 0. Thus to accelerate the CF we want r(n) be be close to ŌĀ(n), and in view of this recursion this means that we want d(n) to be small (but of course nonzero for the formulas of the accelerated CF to make sense). In our situation, we choose continued fractions such that a(n) and b(n) are rational functions or, up to equivalence, polynomials. We thus would like to have our accelerated continued fraction to be of a similar type, and the simplest way to achieve this is to ask for r(n) to be a polynomial, and d(n) to be a nonzero constant, which is of course a rather severe constraint (it is possible to have a weaker condition, see Section B.4 for details). An easy examination of the method shows the following, where we set S(n) = 

p(n)/q (n) and s0 = sign( a0): (1) In the case P ‚ąí, i.e., ő≤ = 2 őĪ + 2 and b0 > 0, we know that S ‚ąí S(n) = 1/[0 , ‚ąí1, 0, |a0|/b 1/20 ]. One can show that we must choose 

r(n) = s0b1/20 nő≤/ 2(1 + ( b1 ‚ąí ő≤/ 2 ‚ąí | a0|/b 1/20 )/(2 n) + ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ ) ,

and then S ‚ąíS‚Ä≤(n) = 1 /[0 , ‚ąí1, 0, |a0|/b 1/20 +2 k] for some k ‚Č• 1, depending on the further coefficients of the expansion of r(n). (2) In the case P +, i.e., ő≤ = 2 őĪ, a20 + 4 b0 = 0, 2 a1 = b1 ‚ąí őĪ, and B > 0 with

4.4. Ap¬ī ery‚Äôs Method 

Ap¬ī ery‚Äôs method can be summarized in two steps: first, do a suitable iteration of the Bauer‚ÄďMuir acceleration method, second use a diagonal procedure on these iterations. The first step proceeds as follows. Assume that at iteration l we have a continued fraction ( a(n, l ), b (n, l )) and partial quotients ( p(n, l ), q (n, l )), so that (a(n, 0) , b (n, 0)) = ( a(n), b (n)), and ( p(n, 0) , q (n, 0)) = ( p(n), q (n)). We choose a suitable sequence r(n, l ), and as above we set d(n, l ) = r(n, l )( r(n + 1 , l ) + a(n +1, l )) ‚ąí b(n, l ). Thanks to the Bauer‚ÄďMuir formulas given above, we can then define a new continued fraction converging to the same limit with coefficients ( a‚Ä≤(n), b ‚Ä≤(n)) and partial quotients ( p‚Ä≤(n), q ‚Ä≤(n)). The first important point is to define the ( l+1)st iteration by setting (a(n, l +1) , b (n, l +1) , p (n, l +1) , q (n, l +1)) = ( a‚Ä≤(n+1) , b ‚Ä≤(n+1) , p ‚Ä≤(n+1) , q ‚Ä≤(n+1)) ,

the shift of n by 1 on the right-hand side being essential. One can then hope to find a general formula for ( a(n, l ), b (n, l )). Finding ( a‚Ä≤(n+1) , b ‚Ä≤(n+1)) in terms of ( a(n), b (n)) is done automatically using the command cfbauer(,1) which in fact returns the four sequences (a,b,r,d) as rational functions. Let us apply this to the first example given above, using the com-mand cftopol which gives the polynomials or rational functions ( a(n), b (n), . . . )for n sufficiently large, omitting the preperiod: 

? v0=cffromser(n->2*n-1,n->-(2*n-1)) % = [[0, 1, 2], [1, 4*n^2 - 4*n + 1]] ? cftopol(v0) % = [2, 4*x^2 - 4*x + 1] ? ab=v0; ? for(l=0,4,abrd=cfbauer(ab,1);print(cftopol(abrd));ab=abrd[1..2]); [6, 4*x^2 - 4*x + 1, 2*x - 3, -4] [10, 4*x^2 - 4*x + 1, 2*x - 5, -16] [14, 4*x^2 - 4*x + 1, 2*x - 7, -36] [18, 4*x^2 - 4*x + 1, 2*x - 9, -64] [22, 4*x^2 - 4*x + 1, 2*x - 11, -100] 

Note that the lth row starting at l = 0 gives ( a(n, l + 1) , b (n, l + 1) , r (n, l ), d (n, l )). We immediately recognize the pattern, so we set 

? a(n,l)=4*l+2; ? b(n,l)=(2*n-1)^2; ? r(n,l)=2*n-2*l-3; ? d(n,l)=-4*(l+1)^2; 

Two remarks: first, the cftopol command used to print the results only gives the polynomial expression for n sufficiently large, so the formulas for a(n,l) etc... just given are valid only for n sufficiently large. This will be important for the second stage of Ap¬ī ery‚Äôs method which we will now study. 4.4. AP ¬īERY‚ÄôS METHOD 31 

The second remark is that this ‚Äúrecognition‚ÄĚ of the pattern for the coefficients is simply polynomial interpolation (in some cases rational function interpolation), and this is done automatically with the cfapery command. The second crucial step in Ap¬ī ery‚Äôs method is to use a diagonal process. Ideally, we would like a continued fraction (in other words a three-term linear recurrence) whose partial quotients are ( p(n, n ), q (n, n )). Although possible, this in general leads to complicated formulas. On the contrary, one can show that if we want a continued fraction of period 2 whose even partial quotients are ( p(n, n ), q (n, n )), and odd partial quotients are ( p(n, n + 1) , q (n, n + 1)), the formulas become extremely simple (and if desired we can obtain the ( p(n, n ), q (n, n )) by contraction). More precisely, we have the following (see Appendix B for details and the proof of a more general result): 

Proposition 4.1 . Set R(n, l ) = a(n + 1 , l ) + r(n + 1 , l ), and for n sufficiently large, set (A(2 n), A (2 n + 1)) = ( R(n, n ‚ąí 1) , R (n, n )) and (B(2 n), B (2 n + 1)) = ( b(n, n ), ‚ąíd(n + 1 , n )) .

Then if we denote by S the limit of the initial continued fraction, the one defined by ( A(n), B (n)) will converge to an expression of the form ( uS + v)/(wS + x) for simple ( u, v, w, x ) corresponding to a correct choice of the initial values of A and 

B.Note that it should be possible to find these correct initial values, but I have been too lazy to do so, since in any case as we will see, they are easy to determine 

a posteriori using the fracdep command, see below. Continuing our example, we thus write: 

? R(n,l)=a(n+1,l)+r(n+1,l); ? AB=[[[R(n,n-1),R(n,n)]],[[b(n,n),-d(n+1,n)]]] % = [[[4*n - 1, 4*n + 1]], [[4*n^2 - 4*n + 1, 4*n^2 + 8*n + 4]]] ? fracdep(cflimit(AB),cflimit(v0)) % = (-x - 1)/x ? cftype(AB)[2] % = [0, -5.828..., 0, 0] 

Note that the command fracdep(U, V) finds if it exists a reasonable size M¬® obius transformation such that V = ( aU + b)/(cU + d), and returns 0 otherwise. Note also the essential double bracket [[...]] to indicate period T = 2. We have thus indeed obtained an exponentially convergent continued fraction with E = ‚ąí(1 + ‚ąö2) 2, which converges to a quantity trivially linked to ŌÄ/ 4. All of this is done automatically by the cfapery command. Let us finish this example by cleaning up ‚Äúby hand‚ÄĚ (which is not done by 

cfapery ): 

? cftochar(AB) % = "-1+1/(1+4/(3+1/(5+16/(7+9/(9+36/(11))))))" ? u=-1+1/(1+4/z);(-u-1)/u % = 1/4*z ? CD=[[2*n+3],[[(2*n+1)^2,(2*n+4)^2]]]; ? cflimit(CD) % = 3.1415926... 32 4. BAUER‚ÄďMUIR‚ÄďAP ¬īERY ACCELERATION 

? cftochar(CD) % = "3+1/(5+16/(7+9/(9+36/(11+25/(13+64/(15))))))" 

We have thus obtained a reasonably simple exponentially convergent continued fraction for ŌÄ.As mentioned, the cfapery command does most of the work. Note, however the following. In the present version of the program (but this may of course change), it outputs AB=[A,B] as above, but with some restrictions. First, it is applicable only to polynomials (as are all of the ones we consider), not rational functions, but only of period 1, second, only if the Bauer‚ÄďMuir accelerations keeps this property, third since, as we have mentioned, the initial values of A(n) and B(n) are not given by general formulas, the limit of the accelerated continued fraction [A,B] is not necessarily equal to that of the initial one but a M¬® obius transformation of it with small integer coefficients (so use fracdep as above to make the correction). Finally, the cftoone command (see below) is done automatically to A and B if applicable since it can only simplify the result, but on the contrary the contraction of the result using cfdoubletosingle (or in fact using cfsimplify , see below) is not done since in general the result would be considerably more complicated. Two additional commands to know which are especially important in the con-text of Ap¬ī ery‚Äôs method are the cfsimplify and cftoone commands. When given a CF of period 2 cfsimplify computes the contraction of ( A, B ) and tries to simplify it (in addition, it tries to find a simpler equivalent CF). The reader can check that applied to our example it gives a new ( A(n), B (n)) with A(n) = 2(4 n + 1)(6 n2 + 3 n ‚ąí 1) and B(n) = ‚ąí(n + 1) 2(2 n + 1) 2(4 n ‚ąí 1)(4 n + 7), not simple enough to supplant our period 2 continued fraction. The cftoone command is different: given a period two A (or B), it checks whether (for n large) the polynomial representing a(2 n + 1) is equal to P (n + 1 /2), where P is the polynomial representing a(2 n), in which case A has in fact period 1 and is thus replaced by the period 1 vector. As we will see in the examples, note that a CF which cannot be transformed into a simpler one using the cfsimplify 

command may be simplified by the cftoone command, and conversely. We now treat our second example, that of an exponentially convergent contin-ued fraction with E = 2 for log(2), and use cfapery directly: 

? ab=cffromser(n->2*n,n->n) % = [[0, 3*n - 1], [1, -2*n^2]] ? cftype(ab)[2] % = [0, 2, 0, 1] /* Convergence in 1/2^n */ ? AB=cfapery(ab) % = [[[0,2],[4*n,4*n+2]],[[1,-2],[-2*n^2,-2*n^2-4*n-2]]] ? cflimit(AB)-log(2) % = 0.E-38 ? cftype(AB)[2] % [0, -5.828..., 0, 0] ? cd=cfsimplify(AB) % = [[0, 6*n - 3], [2, -n^2]] ? cftype(cd)[2] % = [0, 33.97..., 0, 0] ? cftochar(cd) % = "2/(3-1/(9-4/(15-9/(21-16/(27-25/(33))))))" 4.4. AP ¬īERY‚ÄôS METHOD 33 

Here we were doubly lucky: first, Ap¬ī ery acceleration directly gives log(2) and not a M¬® obius transformation of it. Note that starting with an exponentially convergent fraction with E = 2 we obtain one with E = ‚ąí(1 + ‚ąö2) 2 = ‚ąí5.828 ... . Second, the contracted fraction is still extremely simple, and of course the new fraction now has 

E = (1 + ‚ąö2) 4, so we obtain a very simple and very rapidly convergent continued fraction for log(2). We now give a particularly spectacular example of Ap¬ī ery acceleration: 

? ab=[[1/2,7*n-5],[1,-4*n*(3*n-2)]];cftype(ab)[2] % = [0, 4/3, 0, 5/3] /* This is a continued fraction for 2^(1/3) with exponential convergence in C/(4/3)^nn^(5/3). Since 4/3 is rational, we try Apery: */ ? AB=cfapery(ab) % = [[1/2,3,2*n],[[1/2,-5],[-3*n^2+2*n,-3*n^2-8*n-5]]] ? cftype(AB)[2] % = [0, -3, 0, 0] /* Apery works, we have faster exponential convergence in C/(-3)^n. Note that the first component has automatically been simplified to period 1 since cftoone was applicable. Since -3 is rational, we can hope to continue, but we first need to convert to period 1 using cfsimplify: */ ? AB2=cfsimplify(AB) % = [[1/2, 7, 10*n - 5], [2, -9*n^2 + 4]] /* We are in luck, the simplification is much simpler, and evidently with exponential convergence in C/9^n with 9=(-3)^2. Since 9 is rational, we can try Ap\‚Äôery again: */ ? AB3=cfapery(AB2) % = [[1/2,8,18*n-9],[4,-9*n^2+16]]; ? cftype(AB3)[2] % = [0, -33.97..., 0, 0] /* Apery works again, even faster exponential convergence in C/(-(1+sqrt(2))^4)^n. Here, cftoone was in fact applicable to both components, done automatically by cfapery. */ /* Limit is not necessarily equal to 2^(1/3) but to a Moebius transform of it. We use fracdep to find it: */ ? fracdep(cflimit(AB3),2^(1/3)) % = (-10*x - 3)/(12*x - 22) ? %+10/12 % = -32/(18*x - 33) ? cftochar(AB3) % = "1/2+4/(8+7/27-..." ? -10/12-32/(-33+18*(1/2+4/(8+x))) % = (x + 13)/(2*x + 10) ? %-1/2 % = 4/(x + 5) /* Thus, we set */ ? AB4=[[1/2,5,9*(2*n-1)],[4,-(9*n^2-16)]] ? cflimit(AB4)-2^(1/3) 34 4. BAUER‚ÄďMUIR‚ÄďAP ¬īERY ACCELERATION 

% = 0.E-38 

This last CF is given below in the parametric family 5.2.16. It is interesting to note that the first cfsimplify command is really ‚Äúmiracu-lous‚ÄĚ in being so simple, since for instance the exact same procedure applied to the very analogous CF for 2 1/3

ab=[[3/2,7*n-1],[-1,-4*n*(3*n+1)]]; 

would be much more complicated. The main purpose of Ap¬ī ery‚Äôs method is of course to obtain exponentially con-vergent series from a polynomially convergent one, or to improve the exponent from an already exponentially convergent one. We can also use it to obtain polynomially convergent CFs which will usually be completely different. In particular, we have the following (see again the proof in Appendix B): 

Proposition 4.2 . For any fixed m ‚Č• 0, set 

A(n) = R(m + 1 , n ‚ąí 2) + r(m + 1 , n ‚ąí 1) and B(n) = ‚ąíd(m + 1 , n ‚ąí 1) .

Then if we denote by S the limit of the initial continued fraction, the one defined by ( A(n), B (n)) will converge to an expression of the form ( uS + v)/(wS + x) for simple ( u, v, w, x ) corresponding to a correct choice of the initial values of A and 

B.Choosing our first example for ŌÄ/ 4, if we choose m = 0 we find A(n) = 0, so this is not applicable, but for m = 1, we find that A(n) = 4 and B(n) = 4 n2,and an immediate computation using fracdep shows that ŌÄ/ 4 is given by the CF [[1 , 4] , [‚ąí1, 4n2]], which can be simplified using cfmul to [[1 , 2] , [‚ąí1/2, n 2]], in other words 

ŌÄ/ 4 = 1 ‚ąí 1/2/(2 + 1 2/(2 + 2 2/(2 + 3 2/(2 + 4 2/(2 + ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ ))))) (given as 5.3.7), with polynomial convergence ŌÄ/ 4 ‚ąí p(n)/q (n) ‚ąľ (‚ąí1) n+1 ŌÄ/ (32 n2). As mentioned, this is still slow, but is a completely different CF from the initial one (given as 5.3.3) which was [[0 , 1, 2] , [1 , (2 n ‚ąí 1) 2]], in other words 

ŌÄ/ 4 = 1 /(1 + 1 2/(2 + 3 2/(2 + 5 2/(2 + 7 2/(2 + ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ ))))) .

The above is done automatically by the cfaperydual command as follows 

? v0=cffromser(n->2*n-1,n->-(2*n-1)) % = [[0, 1, 2], [1, 4*n^2 - 4*n + 1]] ? cfaperydual(v0) % = [[0, 0], [1, 4*n^2]] /* m = 0, inapplicable */ ? A = cfsimplify(cfaperydual(v0,1)) % = [[0, 2], [1/2, n^2]] /* m = 1, result up to Mobius transformation. */ ? fracdep(cflimit(A),Pi/4) % = -x + 1 ? A = [[1, 2], [-1/2, n^2]] /* We recover the above result. */ 

The last example below is very instructive and shows another aspect of the 

cfaperydual command: 

? v0=cffromser(n->(2*n-1)^2,n->-(2*n-1)^2) % = [[0, 1, 8*n - 8], [1, 16*n^4 - 32*n^3 + 24*n^2 - 8*n + 1]] /* Standard Euler CF from defining series for Catalan‚Äôs constant */ ? A=cfsimplify(cfaperydual(v0)) 4.5. BASIC AP ¬īERY EXAMPLES 35 

% = [[0, 8*n^2 - 8*n + 3], [1/2, -16*n^4]] ? cftype(A) % = [[2, 4, 0, -1/2], [0, 1, 0, 0], 9] /* Very slow logarithmic convergence in C/log(n) */ ? L = cflimit(A) % = 0.91596559417721901481115881008038735296 ? fracdep(L,Catalan) % = 0 ? L - Catalan % = -2.4344... E-19 

First, thanks to the cfaperydual command we obtain a new CF A related to Catalan‚Äôs constant G (in fact as we will see, whose limit is already G itself). First, this CF has extremely slow logarithmic convergence, which is quite exceptional, and second it is possible that it did not appear in the literature before the work of Y. Yang [ 39 ] who used techniques from modular forms. Although the convergence is very slow, the cflimit command still gives an answer using the trick mentioned in Section 3.1: before computing the limit, the program first tries (and succeeds) using a Bauer‚ÄďMuir acceleration cfbauer , which here gives 

[[1,8*n^2-8*n+7],[-1/2,-16*n^4]] 

which now has polynomial convergence P + with P = 2, hence whose limit L can be computed by extrapolation. We now as before try the fracdep command which 

fails . In fact only 19 decimals are correct, which we see in the last command. We could have avoided this apparent failure either by using an additional cfbauer 

command which would now give 33 correct decimals, sufficient to satisfy fracdep ,or by modifying the fracdep command itself by allowing a last argument giving the tolerable decimal accuracy, exactly as for the lindep command. This CF as well as its Bauer‚ÄďMuir accelerated CFs are given as 5.4.27, 5.4.28 and the parametric family therein. Note that exactly the same phenomenon occurs starting from the series 7 ő∂(3) /8 = P 

> n‚Č•1

1/(2 n‚ąí1) 3, and leads to the CFs 5.5.10 and 5.5.11, also discovered by Y. Yang in loc. cit.

4.6. Generalizations of Ap¬ī ery‚Äôs Method 

There are several useful generalizations of Ap¬ī ery‚Äôs method. Although some are implemented in the package, it is instructive to first give the corresponding GP scripts. 

4.6.1. Use of a Simplifier. The first important generalization is the use of a simplifier . Note that the Bauer‚ÄďMuir formulas involve denominators. In nice cases (as all the ones seen above), and in particular when d(n) is constant, these denominators simplify. But in general applying Bauer‚ÄďMuir to a CF with polyno-mial coefficients leads to one with rational function coefficients. In itself this is not too bad, but it becomes catastrophic if we want to iterate , as in Ap¬ī ery‚Äôs method. Now recall that for any CF ( a(n), b (n)) and nonzero function t(n) with t(0) = 1, the CF ( t(n)a(n), t (n)t(n + 1) b(n)) will have the same convergents as the initial CF. This allows us to get rid of the denominators introduced by Bauer‚ÄďMuir. It is immediate to compute the corresponding formulas (see Appendix B for details), and since this modification is not available in the cfapery command, we give the following GP script template, where we recall that tf (n, l ) = Q 

> 1‚Č§m‚Č§n

t(m, l ): 

a(n,l)= b(n,l)= r(n,l)= R(n,l)=a(n+1,l)+r(n+1,l); d(n,l)= 38 4. BAUER‚ÄďMUIR‚ÄďAP ¬īERY ACCELERATION 

t(n,l)= tf(n,l)= /* checks: must be 0 */ r(n,l)*R(n,l)-b(n,l)-d(n,l) t(n,l)*(R(n,l)-r(n-1,l)*d(n,l)/d(n-1,l))-a(n,l+1) t(n,l)*t(n+1,l)*b(n,l)*d(n+1,l)/d(n,l)-b(n,l+1) tf(0,l)-1 tf(n,l)/tf(n-1,l)-t(n,l) VERT(m)=[[0,t(m+1,n-2)*R(m+1,n-2)+r(m+1,n-1)],[1,-t(m+1,n-1)*d(m+1,n-1)]]; DIAG(m)=[[[0,1],[t(n+m,n-1)*R(n+m,n-1),tf(n+m,n)*R(n+m,n)]], [[1,1],[tf(n+m,n)*b(n+m,n),-tf(n+m+1,n)*d(n+m+1,n)]]]; 

This is to be used as follows: a(n, l ), b(n, l ), r(n, l ), and t(n, l ) have to be found ‚Äúby hand‚ÄĚ using the cfbauer command possibly together with a simplifier t(n, l ), followed by polynomial extrapolation. Then d(n, l ) and tf (n, l ) are computed from their definition. One then fills the script with the computed values, and reads it in GP. It must first print five 0‚Äôs, simply to check that the input data is consistent with the recursions and definitions. Then DIAG(0) gives the period 2 main staircase walk (use cfsimplify to see if it can contract to a nice period 1 CF). When t(n, l ) = 1, this is essentially identical to the cfapery command. In some cases there is a division by 0, in which case try DIAG(1) . Similarly VERT(0) gives the main vertical walk, and is essentially identical to the cfaperydual command when t(n, l ) = 1. Note that VERT(m) does not make much sense when the arrays have denominators such as n ‚ąí l or 2 n ‚ąí l since in that case they are not defined for l ‚Č• m or l ‚Č• 2m.To avoid typing the above script, the package provides the function cfaperytemplate 

whose input is the 3 or 5-component vector of polynomials or rational functions in the variables n and l [a(n,l),b(n,l),r(n,l),t(n,l),tf(n,l)] , where the last two may be omitted if equal to 1. Note that it is imperative that the name of the variables be n and l.The output is a two-component vector [VERT,DIAG] of polynomials or rational functions with main variable x, and for instance instead of writing DIAG(0) we must write subst(DIAG,‚Äôx,0) .

4.6.2. Use of a Multiplier. However, it we use a multiplier on the initial CF, the iterations may in some cases become simpler. We illustrate this by computing arrays for the same sums S(k, őĶ ) for other values of ( k, őĶ ), in the same format as above. 

Case (k, őĶ ) = (4 , 1):

4.6.3. Slowing Down Ap¬ī ery. A third generalization of Ap¬ī ery‚Äôs method con-sists in slowing down the acceleration. Evidently here the goal is not to find the fastest possible CF (for instance to prove an irrationality result, as in Ap¬ī ery‚Äôs initial motivation), but simply to find new and hopefully interesting CFs. We illustrate this with log(2), starting again from the standard CF already used in our second example above log(2) = ((0 , 3n ‚ąí 1) , (1 , ‚ąí2n2)). As in the example for ő∂(4), we first use a multiplier, here n, and write the following: 

? CF=[[0,3*n-1],[1,-2*n^2]] ? CF2=cfmul(CF,n) % = [[0,3*n^2-n],[1,-2*n^4-2*n^3]] ? cfbauer(CF2,1)[3] % = [-2,-1,-n^2+n-2] 

This last command tells us that the optimal Bauer‚ÄďMuir function r is r(n) = 

‚ąín2 + n ‚ąí 2. However, let us try something suboptimal and use r(n) = ‚ąín2 + n + r0

for some unknown r0:

? a(n)=3*n^2-n; ? b(n)=-2*n^4-2*n^3; ? r(n)=-n^2+n+‚Äôr0; ? d(n)=r(n)*(a(n+1)+r(n+1))-b(n); ? a1=a(n+1)+r(n+1)-r(n-1)*d(n)/d(n-1); ? b1=b(n)*d(n+1)/d(n); ? res(P)=polresultant(numerator(P),denominator(P),‚Äôn); ? nfroots(,gcd(res(a1),res(b1))) % = [-2, 0, 2]~ 

The rational functions a1 and b1 are the coefficients of the new CF obtained using Bauer‚ÄďMuir. We want them to be as close to polynomials as possible, so computing the roots in r0 of the resultant with respect to the variable n gives the values which give some simplification of a1 and b1 (the command nfroots(,P) 

gives the rational roots of P). The program gives us three possible values of r0: r0 = ‚ąí2 is the optimal Bauer‚Äď Muir, so we can try r0 = 0 or r0 = 2. We can choose any one, so arbitrarily let us decide that at each stage we will choose the largest value of r0.As it happens, this leads to very simple arrays: 

a(n, l ) = 3 n2 ‚ąí n + 2 l(2 n ‚ąí 1) ,b(n, l ) = ‚ąí2n3(n + 2 l + 1) ,r(n, l ) = ‚ąín2 + n + 2( l + 1)(2 l + 1) ,d(n, l ) = 4( n + 2 l + 1)( n + 2 l + 2)( l + 1) 2 ,

and since no denominator occur, t(n,l)=tf(n,l)=1 .4.6. GENERALIZATIONS OF AP ¬īERY‚ÄôS METHOD 41 

We now input this in the template script that we have written above. The vertical walk VERT(0) gives a known CF for log(2). The staircase walk DIAG(0) 

gives a reasonable-looking period 2 CF, but miraculously the cfsimplify command gives a very simple period 1 CF: 

% = [[0,11/2,29*n^2-29*n+8],[5,2,-54*n^4+6*n^2]] 

As usual with Ap¬ī ery the initial terms need to be determined using fracdep ,and we find finally the following new CF which converges like (27 /2) ‚ąín, see 5.3.69: 

[()->log(2),[0,29*n^2-29*n+8],[5,-6*n^2*(9*n^2-1)]] 

A similar procedure leads to the additional new CF which converges like (32 /27) ‚ąín,see 5.3.53: 

[()->log(2),[0,59*n^2-59*n+20],[5,-24*n^2*(36*n^2-1)]] 

See also the remark after 5.3.69. The above procedure can be partly automated thanks to the special flags ‚ąí1and ‚ąí2 in the cfbauer command. For instance, starting again with CF2: 

? CF2=[[0,3*n^2-n],[1,-2*n^4-2*n^3]]; ? VR=cfbauer(CF2,-1) % = [[-n^2+n-2,-n^2+n,-n^2+n+2],\ [[[0,(3*n^3-n^2)/(n-1)],[1,-2*n^4-4*n^3-2*n^2]],\ [[0,3*n^2+3*n],[1,-2*n^4-6*n^3-4*n^2]],\ [[0,3*n^2+3*n-2],[1,-2*n^4-6*n^3]]]] ? R=VR[1][3];CF3=VR[2][3]; VR[1] gives the list of possible r(n), and VR[2] the corresponding new CFs. The flag ‚ąí2 is used when the CF corresponds to a function , so which has extra variables. 

4.6.4. Irrational Ap¬ī ery. We have mentioned that in case of convergence type E with E irrational the use of Bauer‚ÄďMuir and a fortiori Ap¬ī ery would lead to CFs with irrational coefficients. Nonetheless, if we do apply Bauer‚ÄďMuir as a first step for Ap¬ī ery, in many (all?) cases the successive Bauer‚ÄďMuir iterations do not involve any additional irrationalities. I am indebted to T. Stachowiak [ 34 ] for this observation. To take a simple example, starting from the CF 5.3.71: 

[()->log(2),[0,6*n-3],[2,-n^2]] 

which has E = (1 + ‚ąö2) 4 = 33 .97 ... and applying Ap¬ī ery leads to the irrational CF 

[()->log(2),(1+sqrt(2))^2*[0,-4,2*n-1],[16,4,-n^2]] 

with convergence type E = (1 + ‚ąö2) 3(9 + 4 4

‚ąö2 ‚ąí 3‚ąö2) = 133 .87 ... and P = 0. CHAPTER 5 

# Encyclopedic Dictionary: Constants 

5.1. Introduction 

We are now going to give a large list of continued fractions of polynomial type corresponding either to real numbers, or to functions. Before describing the format of the entries, two important warnings are necessary. First, there are literally infinitely many explicit evaluations of infinite series, and many are discovered everyday. Thanks to Euler‚Äôs transformation of series into continued fractions, we thus have an infinite supply of possibly interesting CFs. Therefore, in addition to the CFs that I could find in the literature or the new CFs created for instance using Ap¬ī ery acceleration and Ap¬ī ery duality, I have had to choose among the CFs coming ‚Äútrivially‚ÄĚ from infinite series, essentially keeping only ‚Äúinteresting‚ÄĚ constants and/or CFs, this being of course quite subjective. Second, an important caveat concerning the correctness of the formulas pre-sented in this dictionary. As already mentioned, there are certainly many typos or stupid mathematical errors or wrong signs given in the speed of convergence. But apart from that I must warn the reader about two things: 

‚ÄĘ As mentioned several times, I believe that the indicated speeds of convergence are correct, but since they are based on heuristic proofs and genericity, in some cases they may be wrong, especially when they depend on one or several variables. 

‚ÄĘ But much more importantly: I do not claim that I have proved the correctness of the CFs given here. In some cases, they are either found in the literature, or obtained from those by rigorous methods such as Bauer‚ÄďMuir or Ap¬ī ery. But in other cases, they are obtained ‚Äú` a la Ramanujan‚ÄĚ, by intelligently guessing the general formulas from a few initial terms. It may of course happen that my guesses are incorrect, but I am pretty confident (again modulo typos) that very few will turn out to be wrong. Also, probably in most cases the proofs should not be difficult, but I have not had the patience of doing them. A given entry in the dictionary contains the following information: (1) The mathematical function or constant, given as a Pari/GP closure , even when the corresponding special function or constant does not exist yet. There usually are restrictions on the parameters and variables for the continued fraction to converge to the value of the function, but in many cases these are quite complicated (and in some cases even unknown), so we have not given them. (2) The continued fraction itself in the variable n, in the format given above, except that we add the restriction that all entries have the same length as the period.  

> 43 44 5. ENCYCLOPEDIC DICTIONARY: CONSTANTS

Both the function and the continued fraction are given as a 3-component vector which can be directly input in Pari/GP , or if desired, shortened to a 2-component vector by omitting the first component. (3) A few terms of the continued fraction, for better human visualization. (4) The type and the speed of convergence in F EDP C format, meaning that 

S ‚ąí p(n)

q(n) ‚ąľ Cn!F Ene‚ąöDn nP ,

where S is the value of the continued fraction (in some rare cases, the convergence is very slow and is instead of the form S ‚ąí p(n)/q (n) ‚ąľ

C/ log( n)). The constant C does not make much sense for period 2 CFs so is not given, and in a few rare cases it is not given for some period 1 CFs because we have not found a satisfactory answer. We do not give the asymptotic behavior of q(n) since it is not canonical, and can anyway be retrieved thanks to the functions cftype and cfasymp .(5) A few terms of the more precise asymptotic expansion of S ‚ąí p(n)/q (n)in the form A = 1 + c1/2/n 1/2 + c1/n + ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ , meaning that 

S ‚ąí p(n)

q(n) = Cn!F Ene‚ąöDn nP A . 

To simplify notation, when E is a real quadratic number in Q(‚ąöD) with D

squarefree, the ci are in the same number field, and ‚ąöD is denoted by the variable d. When the CF has period 2, we give the asymptotic expansion 

A of the even convergents and change 2 n into n to make it compatible with period 1. In the case where the number field depends on one or more variables (for example, convergence in ( z + ‚ąöz2 + 1) 2 or similar), determining this asymptotic expansion is painful, and I have not both-ered to do so. Finally, in the special case of convergence of type P + with 

P integral, I do not know of a systematic method to find this expansion with terms 1 /n k with k ‚Č• P , so except in special cases where p(n)/q (n)is completely explicit and given by a simple formula, the asymptotic ex-pansion is only given to P terms (the reader is of course welcome to find additional terms). Since I try to give sufficient information on A, when the entry is A = 1 + ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ or A = 1 ‚ąí 1/n + ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ for instance, this means that I have not been able to find more terms, or possibly that I have not recognized the additional terms. Very often, this means that there exist terms involving log( n). See Appendix A for more details. (6) As mentioned above, in many cases the CF seems to correspond to a reasonably explicit series, which is then given. Note that I do not claim to have proved the correctness of these series. In addition, since the inverse of a CF is just as interesting as the CF itself, if it has a reasonably explicit series it is also given. In fact, many M¬® obius transformations of the CF could give additional series, but for simplicity we have not tried any other than the CF and its inverse. Note that in many cases these series give nontrivial representations of the constant or function. (7) Warning: the sign given in the asymptotics may be incorrect, especially for period 2 CFs, since it is essentially obtained by taking a suitable ‚Äúsquare 5.1. INTRODUCTION 45 

root‚ÄĚ of the corresponding contracted CF. In many cases, it depends on the values of the variables which occur. (8) Evidently a period 2 CF can be contracted (using cfsimplify , which does more) to a period 1 CF. Almost always we will give the latter only if either it has an especially simple form, or if the degrees of the contracted a(n)and b(n) have not increased. (9) In many cases, Bauer‚ÄďMuir‚ÄďAp¬ī ery acceleration of a CF leads to an infinite number of simple CFs polynomially parameterized by an integer constant 

k ‚Č• 0. More generally, one can often find polynomially parameterized CFs for a given constant. It is sometimes quite tedious to find the exact rational expressions which are necessary to specify the first few terms of the CF, such as a(0) and b(0). In this case, we will use a special format, 

not directly readable in GP , of the form [ f, a (n), b (n)], where f is the closure giving the constant or function, and a(n) and b(n) are polynomials representing the coefficients of the CF for n sufficiently large. To illustrate this last point, consider one of the simplest CF for ŌÄ (5.3.7): 

[()->Pi,[4,2],[-2,n^2]] 

It is immediate to use Ap¬ī ery acceleration and to obtain the following (5.3.14): 

[()->Pi,[4,2*n+1],[[-4,9],[(2*n)^2,(2*n+3)^2]]] 

However in doing so, some information is lost since all the intermediate Bauer‚ÄďMuir accelerated CFs are forgotten. In fact, it is easy to see that the kth accelerated CF is 

[()->Pi,[u(k),2*k],[v(k),n^2]] 

where u(k) and v(k) are certain functions of k. We will indicate the parametric family after the CF by 

[()->Pi,2*k,n^2] 

omitting u(k) and v(k) . Unless mentioned otherwise, it is understood that k must be an integer such that k ‚Č• 0: this is especially important since in many cases the 

same parametric family has different limits (or does not converge) when k < 0 or when k / ‚ąą Z: for instance the family 

[k,n^2] 

converges (up to a M¬® obius transformation) to ŌÄ when k is even, and to log(2) when 

k is odd. In this specific example, we have 

u(k) = 4 

> k+1

X

> j=1

(‚ąí1) j

2j ‚ąí 1 and v(k) = ( ‚ąí1) k+1 2 ,

but in other examples the formulas may be more complicated. Note also that using the command cfaperydual on this example leads after simplification to the CF 

[z->Pi,[0,1,2],[4,(2*n-1)^2]] 

which is simply the Euler transformation of the standard series ŌÄ/ 4 = 1 ‚ąí 1/3 + 1/5 ‚ąí ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ , and is given in 5.3.3 below. Note also the trivial fact that if there exists a family [ f (z), a (n), b (n)], meaning that f (z) can be obtained as the limit of a CF with coefficients a(n), b(n) for n46 5. ENCYCLOPEDIC DICTIONARY: CONSTANTS 

sufficiently large, we also have the families [ f (z), a (n + j), b (n + j)] for any integer 

j ‚Č• 0. For a given constant or function, we usually try to give the continued fractions in increasing order of convergence speed, so that the fastest are at the end. Recall that continued fractions ( a, b ) and ( c, d ) are said to be equivalent if their partial quotients p(n)/q (n), are the same, or equivalently if there exists a sequence r(n) with r(n)Őł = 0 such that c(n) = r(n)a(n) and d(n) = r(n)r(n+1) b(n). Among equivalent continued fractions, we have tried to give the ‚Äúsimplest‚ÄĚ one, in particular if possible avoiding unnecessary denominators, except when their use gives a particularly simple continued fraction. Furthermore, when the even (or odd) contraction of a continued fraction is sufficiently simple, we usually only give it and not the initial one. Note also that many of the CFs given for constants are (hopefully interesting) specializations of CFs for functions. 

Example 1 : one of the entries (same as an example given above) reads 

[z->exp(z),[1,n-z],[z,z*n]] 

Calling v this vector, it can be used as follows: 

? r=cflimit(cfsubst(v,z,2/5));s=v[1](2/5);[r-s,cftype(v)] % = [0.E-38, [[1, 1, 0, 0], [1, -1/z, 0, 1], 2]] 

This shows numerically that the limit is indeed exp( z) (at least for z = 2 /5), and that exp( z) ‚ąí p(n)/q (n) ‚ąľ C(z)/(n! ¬∑ (‚ąí1/z )n ¬∑ n), and as above we could compute 

C(z) by using cfasymp .

Example 2 : an entry which initially was in our list was 

[z->exp(z),[1],[z*[1,-1/2],z/(4*n+2)*[1,-1]]] 

The presence of denominators is not pleasing, so to get rid of them we use the function cfmul which gives an equivalent continued fraction, as follows: calling v

the above vector, we write 

? w=cfmul(v,n->[1,4*n+2]) % = [[[1, 2], [1, 4*n + 2]], [[2*z, -z], [z, -z]]] 

This already looks much nicer, but we can also see if the contracted fraction is better: 

? w2=cfdoubletosingle(w) % = [[1, -z + 2, 4*n - 2], [2*z, z^2]] ? cftochar(w2) % = "1+2*z/(-z+2+z^2/(6+z^2/(10+z^2/(14+z^2/(18+z^2/(22))))))" 

We recognize the well-known continued fraction for exp( z), so we do not include the above entry in our list, but only this last formula. Most of the above work can be done automatically by using the cfsimplify 

command. For instance: 

? w2=cfsimplify(v) % = [[1, -z + 2, 4*n - 2], [2*z, z^2]] 

Caveat: Apart from misprints, the CF given in this dictionary should be correct, at least for a suitable range of the variables, usually assumed real. On the other hand, I have usually not given any reference for individual CFs (only global references at the end) since there are so many sources, and also since most 5.2. CONSTANTS: 2 1/3 47 

of them, in particular those obtained by Ap¬ī ery acceleration and by Ap¬ī ery duality, are probably new. Also, please take into consideration the warnings given at the beginning of this chapter. Finally note that most of the constants C given in the asymptotics have been found using linear dependence type algorithms (such as the lindep command), but it may happen that (notwithstanding possible typos) the constant is wrong, although I would really be surprised if it was. However, in most cases it should not be difficult to prove the correctness of the constant, but I have not done so. 

5.2. Constants: 21/3

For many constants, there exist parametric families of (inequivalent) continued fractions, see [ 11 ] for a general discussion. For instance, when Bauer‚ÄďMuir acceler-ation is sufficiently simple to lead to Ap¬ī ery acceleration, this automatically gives a one-parameter family of CFs indexed by the number k of Bauer‚ÄďMuir accelerations, but there are many other types. I give the ones that I have had time or been able to find. Algebraic numbers of degree 2 can be represented by simple (i.e., with b(n) = 1 for all n) periodic CFs. However some other types of CFs are less trivial and perhaps more interesting. I give a single example, where we note that 3 1/2 is a gamma quotient since 31/2 = 2 őď(1 /2) 2

Remarks. 

(1) Although the above is a special cubic polynomial and with the restriction 

|z| > 27 /4, it is not difficult to show that every real root of a cubic polynomial with integer coefficients has a CF expansion of polynomial type as above (but not necessarily a convergent hypergeometric expansion), see [15 ]. (2) Similar CFs exist for roots of trinomials xk +ax +b = 0, and more generally for values of algebraic hypergeometric functions. 

5.3. Constants: ŌÄ, log(2) , and Periods of Degree 1

Preliminaries on CFs for periods : First, we define a rational period of degree k as the sum of a convergent series of the form P 

> n‚Č•1

Ōá(n)f (n), where Ōá is a periodic arithmetic function taking rational values, and f ‚ąą Q(x) is a rational function with rational coefficients, whose denominator is of degree k, or more gen-erally a product of coprime polynomials of degree at most k, one at least having degree k.Note that this definition is not compatible with the definition of periods as given by Kontsevitch‚ÄďZagier [ 27 ]. Note also that a Q-linear combination of rational periods of degree ‚Č§ k is again a rational period of degree ‚Č§ k.By using Euler‚Äôs transformation of series seen above, it is clear that a period has a CF expansion where a(n) and b(n) are polynomials with rational coefficients for 

n large. However this is usually uninteresting, or at least not any more interesting than the series itself. Thus, really interesting CFs are those for which the degrees of the polynomials are reasonably small, specifically deg( a(n)) ‚Č§ k and deg( b(n)) ‚Č§ 2k

for n sufficiently large (we will say that it has bidegree ‚Č§ (k, 2k)). Trivial example: 

S = P 

> n‚Č•1

1/P (n) with deg( P ) = k ‚Č• 2 has the CF S = [[0 , P (n) + P (n ‚ąí

1)] , [1 , ‚ąíP (n)2]] by Euler, of the required degrees. Note that applying Euler and simplifying on the sum S = P

> n‚Č•1

(n + 1) /n 3 = ő∂(3) + ő∂(2) which is a period of degree 3 leads to a CF of bidegree (4 , 8), but there does exist CFs of bidegree (3 , 6) for S, for instance ő∂(3) + ő∂(2) = [[3 , 2n3 + 2 n2 + 3 n + 1] , [‚ąí1, ‚ąín(n + 1) 5]], coming from the identity ő∂(3) + ő∂(2) = 3 ‚ąí P 

> n‚Č•1

1/(n(n + 1) 3). We will however also often give relatively interesting CFs of larger bidegrees, and also of period 2. 5.3.1 .

[()->log(2),3*n^2+(2*(u+v+w)-3)*n+u*v+u*w+v*w-u-v-w+1, -2*n*(n+u)*(n+v)*(n+w)] [()->log(2),3*n^2+(4*(2*u+v+w)+1)*n+4*(u+v)*(u+w), -2*n*(n+u)*(n+2*u+2*v+1)*(n+2*u+2*w+1)] [()->log(2),3*n^2+(4*(u+v+w)-1)*n+2*(2*v-1)*(u+w), -2*n*(n+u)*(n+2*v)*(n+2*u+2*w+1)] [()->log(2),3*n^2+u*(n-1)-1,-2*n^3*(n+u+2)] 

where in the first family u, v, and w must not be all of the same parity. Convergence type E with E = 2 and P = u + v + w, u + 2 v + 2 w + 2, ‚ąíu +2v + 2 w + 1, and ‚ąíu respectively. There exist many other parametric families for log(2) with convergence type

5.8. Constants: Linear Combinations of Zeta- and L-Values 

First note that creating continued fractions for linear combinations of zeta and 

L-values with rational coefficients is considerably easier than for a single such value: for instance, if P (x) and Q(x) are polynomials with rational coefficients such that P

is a product of rational linear factors with no nonnegative integral root, then both P 

> n‚Č•0

Q(n)/P (n) and P

> n‚Č•0

(‚ąí1) nQ(n)/P (n) are such linear combinations when the series converge (deg( P ) ‚Č• deg( Q) + 2 for the former and deg( P ) ‚Č• deg( Q) + 1 for the latter), and can then be converted to CFs using Euler‚Äôs transformation. Nonetheless I believe it useful to include a large list. 

5.8.1. Inhomogeneous Periods. In [ 10 ] we have given three systematic ways of constructing such CFs, and we refer to that paper for the justification of the polynomials Ri(k, n ) which we give below. In the present section we include all the examples from loc. cit., and add many more. A notable characteristic of the present CFs are that their limits are inhomogenous periods, i.e., linear combina-tions of periods of different degrees. As mentioned above, these are much easier to obtain than homogeneous ones, but we give many anyway. In everything that follows, k stands for a nonnegative integer. 

R1(k,n)=(n^k*(2*n+1)+(n-1)^k*(2*n-3))/(2*n-1);

5.8.2. Homogeneous Periods of Degree 1. The next CFs come from con-tinued fractions for Ōą(z), ő≤(z), Ōą‚Ä≤(z), ő≤1(z), Ōą‚Ä≤‚Ä≤ (z), or similar functions. In view of the trivial functional equations giving for instance Ōą(k)(z + 1) ‚ąí Ōą(k)(z), one can trivially construct infinitely many analogous continued fractions from the given ones. See the tables following 6.10.6, 6.11.6, 6.12.3, and 6.13.9 for additional CFs. Note that the limits of the following CFs are now homogeneous periods, i.e., linear combinations of periods of the same degree, contrary to those given above. As already mentioned, these are more difficult to construct than inhomogenous ones. 5.8.20 .
