5.11. Constants Involving Gamma Quotients 

Preliminary remark: if we define the weight w of a gamma quotient by w(őď( a)) = 

a, hence w(ŌÄ1/2) = w(őď(1 /2)) = 1 /2, w(u) = 0 for all algebraic numbers u, and extended additively, then all the gamma quotients occurring in this book have in-tegral weight. For example, you will find CFs for őď(1 /4) 2/‚ąöŌÄ, but not for őď(1 /4) 2

or őď(1 /4) 2/ŌÄ .In addition, please note that all the irrational auxiliary constants which are used are themselves pure gamma quotients, in particular 

‚ąöŌÄ = őď(1 /2) , ‚ąö2 = őď(1 /4)őď(3 /4) őď(1 /2) 2 , ‚ąö2ŌÄ = őď(1 /4)őď(3 /4) őď(1 /2) ,

31/2 = 2 őď(1 /2) 2

őď(1 /3)őď(2 /3) , 21/3 = 2 őď(1 /2)őď(1 /3) őď(1 /6)őď(2 /3) , 22/3 = őď(1 /6)őď(2 /3) őď(1 /2)őď(1 /3) .

Thus, if you find it more aesthetic, in the following CFs you can replace these constants by the corresponding gamma quotients. 

5.11.1. Around the Lemniscatic Constants. There is a wide variety of sometimes conflicting notation related to the so-called lemniscatic constants. We have essentially four closely related constants: the lemniscatic constant ŌĖ, Gauss‚Äô constant G (warning: not to be confused with Catalan‚Äôs constant, also denoted G), and the first and second lemniscatic constants L1 and L2. Here are their expressions in terms of gamma products, recalling that of course őď(1 /4)őď(3 /4) = ŌÄ‚ąö2:

5.13. Other Constants 

The reader will also find some more CFs for constants in the next chapter, obtained by specializing the CFs for functions that we will give. We have included only those who seemed the simplest/nicest, but of course one can give infinitely many. There are evidently also infinitely many variations on the CFs that I have given in this chapter (for instance, I have tried to give some interesting parametric solutions). I would be very interested in knowing genuinely new CFs for interest-ing constants, i.e., not variations of the above nor specializations of the CFs for functions that we give in the next chapter. CHAPTER 6 

# Encyclopedic Dictionary: Functions 

6.1. Trivial Functions with an Arbitrary Sequence 

Let ( f (n)) n‚Č•0 be an (a priori arbitrary) sequence. The CFs in this section converge to a value independent of f , and are valid under suitable assumptions on 

f , which will not be specified since it is difficult to determine the exact domain of validity, but usually for most reasonable f , including for instance non-constant polynomials. All the continued fractions are of the form 

[()->U(2)/U(1),[0,U(n)*f(n)+V(n)], [U(n+2)*f(n+1)+(U(n+2)+V(n)*U(n+1))/U(n)]] 

for suitable functions U and V . The game consists not so much in giving random examples, which have little interest, but in choosing U and V so that the coefficients of the CF are on the one hand polynomials, and second of degree as small as possible. In particular, we can choose deg( V ) < deg( U ). Note that the speed of convergence depends on f so cannot be given, but it is almost always of factorial type. We will thus only give examples, directly taken from [ 7]. Many more examples are of course possible. 6.1.1 .

[()->1,[0,f(n)],[f(n+1)+1]] 

1 = f (1) + 1 

f (1) + f (2) + 1 

f (2) + f (3) + 1 

f (3) + f (4) + 1 

f (4) + f (5) + 1 

f (5) + f (6) + 1 

f (6) + . . .This is the specialization of 6.1.2 to z = 0, k = 1, and f (n) replaced by f (n)+2, and is essentially the only example with deg( U ) = 0. 6.1.2 .

6.3. Exponential Functions 

Preliminary remark: from the point of view of CFs of polynomial type with rational coefficients, which are the only ones considered in this dictionary, the functions f (z) and f (ŌÄz ) (such as exp( z) and exp( ŌÄz ), sinh( z) and sinh( ŌÄz ), tanh( z)and tanh( ŌÄz ) etc...) are quite different in nature. For instance, usually the CFs for 

f (z) converge factorially, while those for f (ŌÄz ) converge only exponentially or even polynomially. 6.3.1 .

6.5. Power Functions

6.6. Direct Trigonometric and Hyperbolic Functions 

Since sin( z) = sinh( iz )/i , cos( z) = cosh( iz ), and tan( z) = tanh( iz )/i , all the continued fractions for trigonometric functions can be deduced trivially from those of hyperbolic functions by replacing z by iz , so we only give the latter. Also, evidently CFs for tanh( z) can be deduced from those of cotanh( z) and conversely. As already mentioned for the exponential functions, there is a complete differ-ence in nature between CFs for f (z) and CFs for f (ŌÄz ), where f is a hyperbolic or trigonometric function. But since we only give CFs with rational coefficients, there is also a difference between CFs for f (ŌÄz ) and CFs for ŌÄf (ŌÄz ): see for instance below the case of f (z) = sinh( z) and f (z) = coth( z). Also, note the following elementary transformation which is applicable to many of the polynomially convergent CFs of this section: 1 + z2

a(1) ‚ąí z2 + . . .= 11 ‚ąí z2

a(1) + . . .(and similarly with z2 replaced by z2 + 1), which can be written using the Pari/GP 

syntax: 

[[1,a(1)-z^2,a(n)],[z^2,b(n)]]=[[0,1,a(n-1)],[1,-z^2,b(n-1)]] 

Thus, every one of these CFs can be written in two (equivalent) ways; we only give the first one. Note finally that since sinh( ŌÄz )/(ŌÄz ) ‚ąí 1

z2 = ŌÄ2

6 + O(z2) and cosh( ŌÄz ) ‚ąí 1

z2 = ŌÄ2

2 + O(z2) ,

6.7. Inverse Trigonometric and Hyperbolic Functions

6.8. Gamma Function 6.8.1. Introduction. General remark: there exist CFs for quotients of prod-ucts of 1, 2, 3, 4, and 8 gamma functions, those for 4 and 8 due to Ramanujan, and those for 3 can be found in [ 19 ]. We give most of them, and refer to [ 19 ] for a few others. Note also that there exist literally thousands of evaluations of hypergeometric series in terms of linear combinations of quotients of gamma functions, which can therefore be trivially converted into CFs for the latter using Euler‚Äôs transformation 406 6. ENCYCLOPEDIC DICTIONARY: FUNCTIONS 

of series into CFs. We have decided not to include them, except the simplest ones, and refer to [ 3] for a large number of examples. See 6.23.1 for a sample. It is convenient to classify CFs related to őď( z) by the number of free variables. Specializing CFs for k variables will of course give CFs for k‚Ä≤ < k variables. We have included CFs for constants (i.e., 0 variable) related to the gamma function in Section 5.11.

Note that the domain of validity (not only of convergence) of this CF is quite complicated and has been partly understood only comparatively recently [ 29 ], so we do not give its speed of convergence. For instance, it is known to be correct if one of the xi for i ‚Č• 1 is an integer (in which case the CF is of course finite). On the contrary, if for instance all the xi are equal to 1 /2, the CF does converge, but not to the expected value. In addition to Ramanujan‚Äôs formulas for ratios of products of 2, 4, and 8 gamma functions, the paper [ 19 ] gives CFs for ratios of products of three gamma functions, of which 6.8.25, 6.8.26, and 6.8.27 are special cases. Almost all the CFs have period 2, except two of them which are similar to Ramanujan‚Äôs. We give one of them (the other is similar but more complicated), referring to [ 19 ] for all the others. 6.16. Ordinary Generating Functions for Bernoulli Type Sequences 

Note that here ‚ÄúOrdinary‚ÄĚ is opposed to ‚ÄúExponential‚ÄĚ, since exponential gen-erating functions for the sequences that we are going to mention are exponential, hyperbolic, or trigonometric functions, whose CFs have been given above in Sec-tions 6.3 and 6.6. 

Definitions and Basic Properties 

For the convenience of the reader, and since some of the sequences are not so well-known (and even possibly new), we recall their definitions and basic properties.

> k‚Č•0

Bkzk. This CF means that if we expand formally the CF as a power series in z, we will find the formal expansion of f (z), i.e. f (z) = 1 ‚ąí z/ 2 + z2/6 ‚ąí z4/30 + ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ , which has radius of convergence 0. Note that this is possible because all the b(n) are divisible by z for n ‚Č• 1. Nonetheless the CF itself converges for zŐł = 1 /2, giving a value to f (z), even though its series diverges (according to 6.11.5, we have f (z) = ‚ąíŌą‚Ä≤(‚ąí1/z )/z when ‚ąí2 < z < 0and f (z) = Ōą‚Ä≤(1 + 1 /z )/z when z > 0 or z < ‚ąí2). 6.19. Jacobi Elliptic Functions: Laplace Transforms 

In what follows, we assume k real with |k| ‚Č§ 1. Since sn( z, k ) vanishes at z = 0, there are no formulas involving sn in the denominator. Since cn( z, k ) vanishes for 

z = K(k) and all odd multiples, formulas involving cn in the denominator are purely formal. Note, however, that the formal (and even non-convergent) CF are still useful, since they give explicitly the Mac-Laurin expansion at 0 of the corresponding elliptic function: if A is the CF in the variable z of the Laplace transform of some elliptic function, the command 

serconvol(exp(z+O(z^N)),cftoser(cfsubst(A,z,1/z),N,z)/z) 

gives the Mac-Laurin expansion at 0. See 6.19.6 below for an explicit example. All of the CFs of this section are of the following two types: either a period 2 CF with constant a(n) (in fact always a(n) = z), and b(2 n), b(2 n + 1) even polynomials of degree at most 2 in k possibly multiplied by z2, (always followed by its even contraction) or a period 1 CF with a(n) = z2 + u(n)k2 + v(n) for suitable 

u(n) and v(n), and b(n) some even polynomial in k of degree at most 4. In the following GP scripts for CFs, it is assumed that all Jacobi elliptic functions 

ellxx are implemented. 6.19.1 .

[(z,k)->intnum(t=0,[oo,z],ellsn(t,k)*exp(-z*t)), [0,z^2+(1+k^2)*(2*n-1)^2],[1,-(2*n-1)*(2*n)^2*(2*n+1)*k^2]] 528 6. ENCYCLOPEDIC DICTIONARY: FUNCTIONS

6.23. General Hypergeometric Functions 6.23.1. Preliminary Remark. Thanks to Euler‚Äôs transformation of hyper-geometric-type series into continued fractions (Proposition 1.7 and Corollary 1.8), any hypergeometric series can trivially be converted into a termwise equal CF. Since there are myriads of specific evaluations of hypergeometric functions (see for instance [ 4]), there exist correspondingly myriad corresponding CFs, and it would be as hopeless a task to give a reasonably complete list as it would be to list all evaluations of hypergeometric functions. We have therefore not even attempted to give even part of such a list. Note that most of the evaluations are linear combinations of quotients of gamma function values. However, I cannot resist giving at least one example of such a (reasonably complicated) CF. It is possible to show the following identity, see [ 3]: 

> 2

F1(2 z, 2z + 1 /3; z + 5 /6; ‚ąí1/8) = (16 /27) z őď( z + 5 /6) /őď(5 /6) őď( z + 2 /3) /őď(2 /3) .

Using Euler, this gives the following CF, which we have not included in our dictio-nary: 6.23.1 .

[(z)->(16/27)^z*gamma(z+5/6)*gamma(2/3)/(gamma(z+2/3)*gamma(5/6)), [1,24*z+20,21*n^2+(12*z+1)*n-2*(2*z-1)*(3*z-1)], [-2*z*(6*z+1),4*n*(2*z+n)*(6*z+3*n+1)*(6*z+6*n-1)]] 

(16 /27) z őď( z + 5 /6) /őď(5 /6) őď( z + 2 /3) /őď(2 /3) = 1 ‚ąí 12 z2 + 2 z

24 z + 20 + 288 z3 + 576 z2 + 376 z + 80 

‚ąí12 z2 + 34 z + 84 + . . .Convergence type E with E = ‚ąí8, P = ‚ąí3z + 3 /2, and C = ... , so that (16 /27) z őď( z + 5 /6) /őď(5 /6) őď( z + 2 /3) /őď(2 /3) ‚ąí p(n)

q(n) ‚ąľ (‚ąí1) n C

# Special Cases 

7.1. Linear Polynomial Type Continued Fractions 

A polynomial type CF ( a(n), b (n)) is linear if a(n) and b(n) are linear (pos-sibly constant) functions of n for n sufficiently large, and we restrict to period 1. We group here the four possible types, depending on the ‚Äúbidegree‚ÄĚ ( da, d b)of ( a(n), b (n)) for n large. If ( da, d b) = (0 , 0), this corresponds to quadratic irra-tionals, nothing very interesting can be said, apart from determining which of the two conjugates of the quadratic irrational ( a + ‚ąöa2 + 4 b)/2 is the limit of the CF

# Asymptotic Expansions and Questions 

A.1. Asymptotic Expansions 

The speed of convergence has been studied in Chapter 2, and in Pari/GP is obtained by the cftype command up to a multiplicative constant, which itself is obtained by the cfasymp()[3][5] command. We may want to obtain further terms in the asymptotic expansion. This is obtained by adding a positive argument L to the cftype command asking for an asymptotic expansion up to 1 /n L (for instance 

cftype(AB,10) ). The purpose of this chapter is to explain how this is implemented. To achieve this, we need four things: first determine the asymptotics of Q

If we want more, note the following: (a) The expansion may involve log( n), as in case of logarithmic conver-gence: in fact, applying Bauer‚ÄďMuir to a CF of type L gives one of type P + with this property. (b) When the expansion is in pure integral powers of 1 /n , it may be possible to compute the convergents explicitly. This is the case in particular when the CF comes from a summation, in which case we can apply the Euler‚ÄďMacLaurin formula. Let us give an example of this last method. A.2. Questions 

In compiling and computing this huge list of continued fractions, a number of sporadic questions have arisen. Here are a few: (1) As mentioned above, it is frustrating that in a single type of convergence (type P + with P integral), I do not know of a systematic method to find the asymptotic expansion A. Closely related is the fact that such asymp-totic expansions may involve log( n), and also have irrational coefficients not related to d, the square root of the discriminant. (2) The CF given as an example for 3 1/2 (5.2.1) has the curious property of being infinitely contractible: usually, computing the contraction of a CF of bidegree ( d1, d 2) gives a CF of larger bidegree. For the present CF of bidegree (2 , 4), the contractions become considerably more complicated, but can always be simplified (i.e., to a CF with the same partial quotients) to bidegree (2 , 4). First, I do not know how to prove this, and second, I do not know how to characterize such CFs. (3) The parametric families, especially those for periods of degree 2 and 3, have been obtained by searches. In many cases they are probably not complete, but in some cases there are so many that it would be desirable to classify them, which I have not always succeeded in doing. See [ 11 ] for detailed discussions of this. (4) In the case of logarithmic convergence (type L), I do not know of any reasonable method to compute the constant C such that S ‚ąí p(n)/q (n) ‚ąľ

C/ log( n), except when the CF is completely explicit, or when it is the specialization of a CF with more variables (for instance, this is how I found that the constant C for 5.4.27 is equal to ŌÄ3/8 and that of 5.5.10 is

B.2. Contraction 

Note that we have set u‚Ä≤(n) = u(n + 1) + r(n)u(n) instead of the possibly more natural u(n) + r(n ‚ąí 1) u(n ‚ąí 1): the reason for this is that this is what we need for Ap¬ī ery acceleration, but has the effect of creating a continued fraction of length 

n + 1 from the first n terms. We thus need to contract it to a CF of length n, not by changing n + 1 to n, nor by contracting the last convergents (this could not be used in Ap¬ī ery), but by contracting the first convergents. This is done by using the following trivial lemma:

However, in view of the Bauer‚ÄďMuir formulas, if we want polynomial coefficients (which of course will rarely be possible), since d(n)is coprime to d(n + 1), we must have d(n) | b(n) and d(n) | r(n), which gives a condition. We thus proceed as follows: if b(n) does not have a degree 1 divisor (over 

Q), nothing much can be done, choose for instance r(n) = ( ‚ąía0/2) nőĪ. If b(n) does have a degree 1 divisor, say b(n) = ( un + v)c(n) with deg( c) = ő≤ ‚ąí 1 monic, we must have d(n) = d0c(n) for some constant d0, and hence r(n) = ( ‚ąía0/2) c(n) since 

c is monic, which implies that őĪ = deg( r) = ő≤ ‚ąí 1 = 2 őĪ ‚ąí 1, in other words this can happen only when őĪ = 1 and ő≤ = 2, and b(n) a second degree polynomial which factors into linear factors. We can redo the computation: r(n) = ( ‚ąía0/2)( n + r1)with ‚ąír1 one of the roots of b(n), a(n) = a0n+a0a1, b(n) = ( ‚ąía20/4)( n+r1)( n+u), say, so 

d(n) = ( a20/4)( n + r1)( r1 + u ‚ąí 2a1 ‚ąí 1) .

This is always divisible by r(n), but we also need it to be nonzero: note that r1 + u

is the opposite of the sum of the roots of b(n), and since b(n) = b0(n2 + b1n + b2)we have r1 + u = b1, so r1 + u ‚ąí 2a1 ‚ąí 1 = b1 ‚ąí 2a1 ‚ąí 1 < 0 never vanishes. To summarize, in case D+ we can find a new polynomial type continued fraction if and only if őĪ = 1, hence ő≤ = 2, and b(n) factors into two linear factors over Q,in which case one must choose r(n) = ( ‚ąía0/2)( n + r1), where n + r1 is one of the linear factors of b(n). 

B.4. Appendix: Gory Details III: Ap¬ī ery Acceleration 

The main idea is to iterate Bauer‚ÄďMuir transforms, and to take a diagonal or pseudo-diagonal. We must find the necessary formulas. Let ( a(n), b (n), p (n), q (n)) be an initial CF, which we will consider as our 0th it-eration, so set ( a(n, 0) , b (n, 0) , p (n, 0) , q (n, 0)) = ( a(n), b (n), p (n), q (n)), and assume by induction that at the l-th iteration we have the recursion 

u(n + 1 , l ) = a(n + 1 , l )u(n, l ) + b(n, l )u(n ‚ąí 1, l ) ,

where here and below u stands for p or q; by assumption this is true for l = 0. In view of the Bauer‚ÄďMuir formulas we set 

R(n, l ) = r(n + 1 , l ) + a(n + 1 , l ) and 

d(n, l ) = r(n, l )R(n, l ) ‚ąí b(n, l ) .604 B. IMPLEMENTATION OF BAUER‚ÄďMUIR‚ÄďAP ¬īERY 

Now the Bauer‚ÄďMuir formulas have denominators (unless, for instance, d(n, l )is independent of n), so we introduce a third function t(n, l ), and define 

a(n, l + 1) = t(n, l )( R(n, l ) ‚ąí r(n ‚ąí 1, l )d(n, l )/d (n ‚ąí 1, l )) ,b(n, l + 1) = t(n, l )t(n + 1 , l )b(n, l )d(n + 1 , l )/d (n, l ) ,u(n, l + 1) = t!( n, l )( u(n + 1 , l ) + r(n + 1 , l )u(n, l )) ,

the factorial being of course taken as a function of n.Then an easy check shows that we indeed have the recursion 

u(n + 1 , l + 1) = a(n + 1 , l + 1) u(n, l + 1) + b(n, l + 1) u(n ‚ąí 1, l + 1) ,

which is thus valid for all l.We would now like to take the diagonal, i.e., find a recursion for ( p(n, n ), q (n, n )). This is possible, but the formulas again involve denominators, exactly the same as those occurring when contracting a CF. Thus, as we will see, it is much cleaner to use staircase steps instead of direct diagonal ones. In other words, we will find a 3-term recursion between on the one hand ( u(n ‚ąí 1, n ‚ąí 1) , u (n, n ‚ąí 1) , u (n, n )), and on the other hand ( u(n, n ‚ąí 1) , u (n, n ), u (n + 1 , n )), and putting both together we will obtain a period 2 CF, in that the even and odd coefficients are given by different formulas. As for the Bauer‚ÄďMuir formulas, this is simply done by solving trivial linear systems. Before embarking on this, let us recall that our recursions are as follows: 

u(n + 1 , l ) = a(n + 1 , l )u(n, l ) + b(n, l )u(n ‚ąí 1, l ) and 

u(n, l + 1) = t!( n, l )( u(n + 1 , l ) + r(n + 1 , l )u(n, l )) 

Proposition B.4 . We have the recursions 

u(n, l + 1) = t!( n, l )( R(n, l )u(n, l ) + b(n, l )u(n ‚ąí 1, l )) ,u(n + 1 , l + 1) = t(n + 1 , l )R(n + 1 , l )u(n, l + 1) ‚ąí t!( n + 1 , l )d(n + 1 , l )u(n, l ) ,u(n, l + 1) = t!( n, l )( A(n, l )u(n, l ) + B(n, l )u(n, l ‚ąí 1)) , with 

A(n, l ) = t(n + 1 , l ‚ąí 1) R(n + 1 , l ‚ąí 1) + r(n + 1 , l ) and 

B(n, l ) = ‚ąít!( n + 1 , l ‚ąí 1) d(n + 1 , l ‚ąí 1) .

Proof. We have 

u(n, l + 1) = t!( n, l )( u(n + 1 , l ) + r(n + 1 , l )u(n, l )) = t!( n, l )(( a(n + 1 , l )u(n, l ) + b(n, l )u(n ‚ąí 1, l )) + r(n + 1 , l )u(n, l )) = t!( n, l )R(n, l )u(n, l ) + t!( n, l )b(n, l )u(n ‚ąí 1, l ) ,

so the first recursion is immediate. For the second, setting x = u(n + 1 , l ) we have 

u(n + 1 , l + 1) = t!( n + 1 , l )( u(n + 2 , l ) + r(n + 2 , l )x)= t!( n + 1 , l )(( a(n + 2 , l )x + b(n + 1 , l )u(n, l )) + r(n + 2 , l )x)= t!( n + 1 , l )( R(n + 1 , l )x + b(n + 1 , l )u(n, l )) ,

and since u(n, l + 1) = t!( n, l )( x + r(n + 1 , l )u(n, l )) we have 

x = u(n, l + 1) /t !( n, l ) ‚ąí r(n + 1 , l )u(n, l ) ,B.4. APPENDIX: GORY DETAILS III: AP ¬īERY ACCELERATION 605 

so 

u(n + 1 , l + 1) = t!( n + 1 , l )( R(n + 1 , l )( u(n, l + 1) /t !( n, l ) ‚ąí r(n + 1 , l )u(n, l )) + b(n + 1 , l )u(n, l )) = t(n + 1 , l )R(n + 1 , l )u(n, l + 1) 

‚ąí t!( n + 1 , l )( r(n + 1 , l )R(n + 1 , l ) ‚ąí b(n + 1 , l )) u(n, l )= t(n + 1 , l )R(n + 1 , l )u(n, l + 1) ‚ąí t!( n + 1 , l )d(n + 1 , l )u(n, l ) ,

proving the second recursion. For the third, we have

proving the third recursion. ‚Ė°

We can now play with different paths giving new CFs. 

Corollary B.5 . (Vertical walk). Let m ‚Č• 0 be fixed, and for n ‚Č• 2 set 

aV (n) = t(m + 1 , n ‚ąí 2) R(m + 1 , n ‚ąí 2) + r(m + 1 , n ‚ąí 1) and

# Hypergeometric Series 

C.1. Introduction C.1.1. Interesting Series. As already mentioned, in compiling the database of CFs given in this book it was found partly experimentally that a substantial proportion of these continued fractions correspond in fact to generalized hypergeo-metric series through Euler‚Äôs transformation of series into continued fractions. The purpose of this chapter is to give a list of the most interesting ones. Note that I do not claim to have proved all of them. The notion of ‚Äúinteresting‚ÄĚ series is completely subjective, so here is a non-exhaustive list of the series that I do not include. 

‚ÄĘ Rational series P 

> n‚Č•0

P (n)/Q (n) where Q(x) is a polynomial having only rational roots. Indeed, by expanding into partial fractions the sum of such a series can be computed explicitly, so is not interesting. When Q(x) has irreducible factors of degree at least 2, it is also easy to sum the series in terms of complex values of polygamma functions, but the series are already more interesting so we keep them, except when they sums to less interesting constants or functions, such as linear combinations of zeta or L-values. 

‚ÄĘ Series comming by trivial specializations of classical power series. For instance the series log(2) = P 

> n‚Č•0

3‚ąí2n‚ąí1/(n + 1 /2) is uninteresting since it simply comes from the Taylor series expansion of ‚ąí log(1 ‚ąí x) for x = 1 /9. 

‚ÄĘ Series which are trivially equivalent to the definition, for instance e = 1 + P 

> n‚Č•0

1/(n + 1)! or Ōą‚Ä≤(z) = P 

> n‚Č•1

1/(n + z ‚ąí 1) 2, or correspond to well-known expansions such as atanh( z) = P 

> n‚Č•0

z2n+1 /(2 n + 1). 

C.1.2. Use of the Pochhammer symbol. Although all the series could be expressed as hypergeometric evaluations, I have preferred to give the series more explicitly using the rising Pochhammer symbol ( a)n = a(a + 1) ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ (a + n ‚ąí 1) = őď( a + n)/őď( a), and replacing expressions such as ( a + k)n/(a)n or (its inverse) with 

k ‚ąą Z by rational functions. Several remarks: (1) When a is a positive integer, ( a)n is (up to a multiplicative constant) a factorial, so we will write n!, ( n + 1)!, and ( n + 2)! /2 instead of (1) n, (2) n,or (3) n respectively. (2) When a is half an odd integer, we will keep the notation as is, but of course note that (1 /2) n = (2 n)! /(2 2nn!), (3 /2) n = (2 n + 1)! /(2 2nn!), and so on. 

C.1.3. Numerical Check of the Formulas. It is of course essential to be able to check numerically the given formulas. Using Pari/GP (and evidently other systems) this is very easy but uses an essential trick.

> 1‚Č§i‚Č§g

In Pari/GP this is the command suminf .(2) If E = 0 and w = ‚ąí1, use the command sumalt , very efficient for summing alternating series (note that there are no series in our list with |w| = 1 and wŐł = ¬Ī1). (3) The more difficult case is when E = 0 and w = 1. Here, one must use the powerful discrete Euler‚ÄďMacLaurin summation explained in [ 1] and available in Pari/GP under the name sumnum . It works very well but must be used with care: The standard syntax is sumnum(n=a,f(n)) . This is OK if f (n) is for instance a rational function of n. But if f involves Pochhammer symbols, this cannot work for several reasons. The first essential reason is that sumnum uses noninte-gral values of n, so defining poch(a,n)=prod(j=0,n-1,a+j) , or using n! would not work. Instead, one can define poch(a,n)=gamma(a+n)/gamma(a) , and replace 

n! by gamma(n+1) . But even that is not sufficient. Consider for example the following series: 

S = X

> n‚Č•0

(3 /2) n

(2 n + 3)( n + 1)! ,

whose value is ŌÄ ‚ąí 2. Writing 

S=sumnum(n=0,gamma(n+3/2)/(gamma(3/2)*(2*n+3)*gamma(n+2))) 

results in an error message ‚Äúoverflow in expo()‚ÄĚ, because both the numerator and denominator overflow for large values of n. Thus a more reasonable command is 

S=sumnum(n=0,exp(lngamma(n+3/2)-lngamma(n+2))/(2*n+3))/gamma(3/2) 

This seems to work but gives a completely wrong answer. The reason is because internally n is used as a real number with insufficient accuracy. The solution if to write the following (assuming we are working at 38 decimals of accuracy): 

S=sumnum(n0=0,my(n=precision(n0,77)); exp(lngamma(n+3/2)-lngamma(n+2))/(2*n+3))/gamma(3/2) 

and now the result is correct to 31 decimal digits, not perfect but sufficient for numerically checking the formula.

C.4. An Observation on Gamma Quotients

a1b1APPENDIX D 

# The Pari/GP File of Continued Fractions 

This chapter is more or less the same as [ 12 ], whose source code should be downloaded in any case to have the database. It would be tedious to copy the 1860 or so continued fractions one by one to be able to work on them. In addition, one may want to do searches for CFs of a certain type, or other properties. For this, we provide a single file, let us call it ‚Äúallcf.gp‚ÄĚ, readable by Pari/GP with the command 

V=readvec("allcf.gp"); 

which allows to have a complete database of all the CFs given in this book, together with their main properties. To obtain this file, download the TEX source of [ 12 ] and create the file ‚Äúallcf.gp‚ÄĚ by extracting everything between the last two comments 

of the source. The aim of the present chapter is to describe in detail the format of this data-base and how it is used. Once again, it is essential to have the henri-ellCF2 

branch of Pari/GP installed, although of course the format being transparent can be used with any other software through an appropriate interface. First, a warning. We reserve the following variable names which must never be used in working with the CFs of this file: (1) ‚Äún‚ÄĚ, which is universally used to denote the CF index. (2) ‚Äúd‚ÄĚ, which denotes the radicand of a quadratic extension, see below the explanation for v[3][1] .(3) ‚Äúx‚ÄĚ which stands for 1 /n and ‚Äúy‚ÄĚ which stands for 1 /n 1/2 in the asymp-totic expansions A, see the explanation for v[3][4] .There are three kinds of inputs in the file. By far the most important ones are of course the CFs themselves, which are also the most numerous. 

‚ÄĘ Format of the Continued Fractions Entries 

Such an entry, let‚Äôs call it v, has eight components, which we now describe. To make the explanation clearer, we use the following example: 

[(z)->cosh(Pi*z),[[1,1/2,4*n^2-8*n+(2*z^2+5)], [2*z^2,-4*n^4+8*n^3+(-4*z^2-6)*n^2+(4*z^2+2)*n+(-z^2-1/4)]], [1,[0,1,0,1],(z)->z^2*cosh(Pi*z), 1-1/2*z^2*x+(1/6*z^4-1/6*z^2-1/12)*x^2+(-1/24*z^4+1/6*z^2+1/12)*x^3+O(x^4)], [[1,4*z^2,[1/2-I*z,1;1/2+I*z,1;3/2,-2],1], [1,-4*z^2,[-1/2,2;1/2-I*z,-1;1/2+I*z,-1],1]], [],"3.2.4",["AP->3.2.5","APD->3.2.4.5"],""]; 

(1) v[1] is a closure giving the limit of the CF. Note that in CFs involving variables, the CF may converge to that limit only in a certain range of the variables, but we have not given that (we may in a future version).  

> 643 644 D. THE PARI/GP FILE OF CONTINUED FRACTIONS

(2) v[2] is the [ a(n), b (n)] of the CF in the format explained above, with one important difference: consider for instance the entry 1.5.10 beginning with: 

[k->zeta(k),[0,n^k+(n-1)^k],[1,-n^(2*k)]] 

Giving this to GP would make it choke (give an error message) because 

n^k is not a valid GP input when k is a variable. Thus we replace it in the file by 

[k->zeta(k),[k->[0,n^k+(n-1)^k],k->[1,-n^(2*k)]]] 

which is a valid input since the closure is not evaluated. (3) v[3] is itself a 4-component vector [d,[F,E,D,P],C,A] giving the exact speed of convergence of the CF, including a few terms of its asymptotic expansion. If L denotes the value of the CF and p(n)/q (n) are the partial quotients, this notation means that 

L ‚ąí p(n)

q(n) = Cn!F Ene‚ąöDn nP A , 

with the following conventions: (a) v[3][1] is a number or a polynomial (very often 1) with the fol-lowing meaning: often, the speed of convergence and the asymptotic expansions involve elements of a quadratic extension (of Q or of Q[z]for instance). To avoid using square roots, we set v[3][1] to the radicand, and we use the specific letter d to indicate the square root of v[3][1] . Thus, we must avoid the letter d in variables of CFs. In the running example, we have v[3][1]=1 so no quadratic extension. (b) v[3][2] gives the speed of convergence the above FEDP format, using d if necessary. The running example has v[3][2]=[0,1,0,1] ,meaning that we are in case P + with P = 1. In some rare cases, the speed does not make any sense, in which case we set v[3][2]=0 . In some other rare cases, the CF does not converge for real values of the variables (which is implicitly what is assumed), in which case we set 

v[3][2]=[0,0,0,0] .(c) v[3][3] gives the constant C such that S ‚ąíp(n)/q (n) ‚ąľ C/ [F EDP ], or 0 if unknown or has not been computed. Note that C is always given as a closure, first because in some cases (as for the CF itself) it is not possible to do otherwise in GP , and second because writing 

C=()->Pi is both much clearer to read than C=3.1415... and also allows to have C to any accuracy. (d) v[3][4] gives the asympotic expansion A=1+... , such that S ‚ąí

p(n)/q (n) = ( C/ [F EDP ]) A, or 0 if unknown or not computed. The reserved letter x means 1 /n and the reserved letter y means 1 /n 1/2.Thus, the above example says that cosh( ŌÄz ) ‚ąí p(n)

q(n) = z2 cosh( ŌÄz )

n



1 ‚ąí z2/2

n + z4/6 ‚ąí z2/2 ‚ąí 1/12 

n2 + ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ 



(4) In a large number of cases, the CF or its inverse corresponds to a more or less explicit hypergeometric series S given in a special format explained below. Thus, v[4] is set to 0 if neither CF nor its inverse has such a series, to S if only the CF has a series, to the two-component vector D. THE PARI/GP FILE OF CONTINUED FRACTIONS 645 

[S,T] if both have a series (with T the series for the inverse), and finally the two-component vector [[],T] if only the inverse has a series. These series are given in the following format: they are all of the form 

S = u + X

> n‚Č•0

h(n) Y

> 1‚Č§i‚Č§g

(ai)ei

The fact that the series begins at n = 0 has been chosen for uniformity, but often leads to slightly more cumbersome formulas, for instance instead of ő∂(2) = P 

> n‚Č•1

1/n 2 we write instead ő∂(2) = P 

> n‚Č•0

1/(n + 1) 2, hence represented by [0,1/(n+1)^2,1,1] .(5) v[5] is used for testing. When empty, it means that the default values for the variables used in the CF can be used (presently 2 /3, 3 /4, 1 /2, 2 for closures with arity less or equal to 4). If it is a vector of values, these are taken instead of the default values. For instance for a closure of arity 2, v[5]=[2/3,-1] means that one can take the default value 2 /3 for the first variable, but probably cannot use 3 /4 for the second, but ‚ąí1 instead. Other values of v[5] have specific meanings which may change: 

‚ÄĘ v[5]=1 : Bernoulli type sequence, test expansion. 

‚ÄĘ v[5]=2 : Jacobi type sequence, only test expansion. 

‚ÄĘ v[5]=3 : Jacobi type sequence, test expansion and numerics, (2,1/2). 

‚ÄĘ v[5]=4 : Change z into 1/z, multiply by z, test expansion. 

‚ÄĘ v[5]=5 : Choose z = 2 i, multiply by ‚ąíi, test. Note that in the present version, v[5]=4 and v[5]=5 occur each for a single CF. (6) v[6] is the TEX label used for the CF, so as to be able to find it easily. It is very useful to write a trivial little script to find conversely the given entry of the big vector V corresponding to a given label. Note that the labels are rather arbitrary (but of course unique) since they were given after discovery of new CFs, and more or less in increasing order. (7) v[7] is a 2-component vector [A1,AD1] whose entries are strings describ-ing the behavior of the CF under Ap¬ī ery acceleration: A1 is for the Ap¬ī ery diagonal process, and AD1 for the Ap¬ī ery dual. The meanings of the strings are as follows: ‚ÄúNO‚ÄĚ if Ap¬ī ery is not applicable, ‚ÄúBAcomp‚ÄĚ if it is but the Bauer‚ÄďMuir method introduces complicated denominators, ‚ÄúAP ‚Üílabel‚ÄĚ (‚ÄúAPSI ‚Üílabel‚ÄĚ if followed by a simplification) and ‚ÄúAPD ‚Üílabel‚ÄĚ if Ap¬ī ery leads to the corresponding labeled CF, (‚ÄúAPD ‚ÜíSELF‚ÄĚ it is self-dual), 646 D. THE PARI/GP FILE OF CONTINUED FRACTIONS 

‚ÄúAPcomp‚ÄĚ and ‚ÄúAPDcomp‚ÄĚ if the result is too complicated to be in-cluded in the encyclopedia so with no label, and a possible additional ‚Äúsim‚ÄĚ if similar to. (8) v[8] is a (usually empty) string giving any additional information. 

‚ÄĘ Format of Parametrized Continued Fractions 

Many CFs are part of families of inequivalent CFs depending on one or more parameters. These families are included in the file in a different format, since the initial values of a(n) and b(n) are not given. We have only given such families for CFs of period 1. The corresponding vector v has now only five components, and again to make the explanation clearer we give an example: 

[[()->Pi,2*k+2,(2*n-1)*(2*n+2*k-4*u-1)] [1,[0,-1,0,k+1]],"1.2.1",[],"u<=k/2"]; 

(1) The first component v[1] is a three-component vector: v[1][1] is the closure giving the limit S, and v[1][2] and v[1][3] are polynomials giving a(n) and b(n) for n sufficiently large. This means that for suitable initial values a(0) , a (1) , . . . , and b(0) , b (1) , . . . , and for given nonnegative integers k and u, the corresponding CF converges to S. Equivalently, the CF [[v[1][2]],v[1][3]] converges to a limit of the form ( aS + b)/(cS +

d) with a, b, c, d integral and ad ‚ąí bc Őł = 0. For instance, choosing u = 0 and k = 0 , 1, 2, or u = 1 and k = 2, the above parametrized CF says that the limits of the CFs 2 + 1 /(2 + 1 /(2 + 9 /(2 + 25 /(2 + 49 /(2 + 81 /(2 + ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ )))))) ,

4 ‚ąí 1/(4 + 3 /(4 + 15 /(4 + 35 /(4 + 63 /(4 + 99 /(4 + ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ )))))) ,

6 ‚ąí 3/(6 + 5 /(6 + 21 /(6 + 45 /(6 + 77 /(6 + 117 /(6 + ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ )))))) , and 6 + 1 /(6 + 1 /(6 + 9 /(6 + 25 /(6 + 49 /(6 + 81 /(6 + ¬∑ ¬∑ ¬∑ )))))) all belong to Q(ŌÄ). (2) v[2] contains the information on the speed of convergence in the short-ened format [d,[F,E,D,P]] , since the constant C and the asymptotic expansion A vary in a parametric family so are not given. (3) v[3] is the label where the parametric CF occurs (same as v[5] for ordi-nary CFs). (4) v[4] is used for testing, but for now is the empty vector. (5) v[5] is a string giving some additional information. Note that for integer k, the CF ( a(n + k), b (n + k)) is essentially the same as the CF ( a(n), b (n)), so is of course not considered as a new CF, so this trivial parametrization is not included. 

‚ÄĘ Format of Definitions 

In some of the CFs, it is necessary to add some function definitions, either be-cause the corresponding functions do not exist in Pari/GP (for example bernfrac(k) 

and eulerfrac(k) exist, but not tanfrac(k) for the tangent numbers), or simply because the existing expression is cumbersome. The command V=readvec("file.gp"); 

will have two effects: first, it defines the function so that it can be used in the CFs, and second it is kept as an element of V as a closure (but without its name). Thus, D. THE PARI/GP FILE OF CONTINUED FRACTIONS 647 

if you do not know the meaning of a function, typing ?functionname will give you an explanation or a formula for it. 

More Details 

Here is in more detail the different types of vectors v that we can encounter, with examples. (1) Definitions: as explained above. These are closures . Example: 

R1(k,n)=(n^k*(2*n+1)+(n-1)^k*(2*n-3))/(2*n-1); 

(2) Parametrized continued fractions: as explained above. These are vectors 

with five components. Example: 

[[()->2^(1/3),7*n-5+k+v+4*u,-4*(n+u)*(3*n+v-2)], [1,[0,4/3,0,u+(5-v)/3+2*k]],"1.1.1",[],""]; 

(3) Ordinary CF with a or b expressed as a closure: These are vectors v with 

eight components such that v[2][1] or v[2][2] is a closure. Example: 

[k->zeta(k),[k->[0,n^k+(n-1)^k],k->[1,-n^(2*k)]],[1,[0,1,0,k-1], k->1/(k-1),1-((k-1)/2)*x+sum(m=1,4,binomial(k+2*m-2,2*m)* bernfrac(2*m)*x^(2*m),O(x^10))],0,[3],"1.5.10",["NO","NO"],""]; 

(4) Ordinary CF with the last component of a and b a closure. Example: 

[()->1,[[0,n->f(n)],[n->f(n+1)+1]],[0,0,0,0], 0,[],"2.1.0.1",["NO","NO"],""]; 

(5) Ordinary CF of period 2 or more: These are vectors v with eight compo-nents such that the first (hence any) component of v[2][1] or v[2][2] is a vector. Example: 

[()->2^(1/3),[[[0,5],[2*n,12*n+6]],[[6,-1/3],[-(n+1)*(3*n+2), -n*(3*n+1)]]],[2,[0,(1+d)^2,0,0],()->2^(1/3)*3^(1/2)/(1+sqrt(2))^2, 1+(9*d/8)*x+(-9*d/8+81/64)*x^2+(67105*d/41472-81/32)*x^3+O(x^4)], 0,[],"1.1.6",["NO","NO"],""]; 

(6) Ordinary CF of period 1 with no closures. Example: 

[()->3^(1/2),[[3/2,(2*n+1)^2],[2,-n^2*(n+2)^2]],[3,[0,(2+d)^2,0,0], ()->2*sqrt(3)/(2+sqrt(3))^3,1+d*x+(3/2-3*d/2)*x^2+(3*d-9/2)*x^3 +(45/4-27*d/4)*x^4+O(x^5)],0,[],"1.1.0.5", ["NO","NO"],"Infinitely contractible"]; 

A second example with Ap¬ī ery information and series for the inverse: 

[()->2^(1/3),[[1/2,7*n-5],[1,-12*n^2+8*n]],[1,[0,4/3,0,5/3], ()->2^(8/3)/gamma(1/3),1-55/9*x+170/3*x^2-1579490/2187*x^3 +230596751/19683*x^4+O(x^5)],[[],[0,2,[-2/3,1;1,-1],3/4]], [],"1.1.1",["APSI->1.1.8","APD->SELF"],""]; 

where the string ‚ÄúAPSI ‚Üí1.1.8‚ÄĚ indicates that after simplification, Ap¬ī ery leads to label 1.1.8, and ‚ÄúAPD ‚ÜíSELF‚ÄĚ that it is self-dual for Ap¬ī ery. The series entry says that 2 ‚ąí1/3 = 2 P

> n‚Č•0

(( ‚ąí2/3) n/n !)(3 /4) n, which is simply the special case x = 3 /4 of the expansion of 2(1 ‚ąí x)2/3.Here is a little script which outputs a number from 1 to 6 giving the type of a vector v:648 D. THE PARI/GP FILE OF CONTINUED FRACTIONS 

/* Find the type number of an entry in the file */ typevec(v)= { my(tv=type(v),lv=#v,a,b); if(tv=="t_CLOSURE",return(1)); if(tv!="t_VEC"||(lv!=5&&lv!=8),error("incorrect entry")); if(lv==5,return(2)); [a,b]=v[2]; if(type(a)=="t_CLOSURE"||type(b)=="t_CLOSURE",return(3)); if(type(a)!="t_VEC"||type(b)!="t_VEC",error("incorrect entry 2")); if(type(a[#a])=="t_CLOSURE"||type(b[#b])=="t_CLOSURE",return(4)); if(type(a[1])=="t_VEC"||type(b[1])=="t_VEC",return(5)); return(6); } 

Here is a script which outputs the bidegree for ordinary CFs of period 1 with no closures, and -1 otherwise. 

bidegree(v)= { if(typevec(v)!=6,return(-1)); return(apply(poldegree,cftopol(v))); } findbidegree(da,db)= { my(V=readvec("allcf.gp")); for(i=1,#V,my(v=V[i]);if(bidegree(v)==[da,db],print1(i," "))); } Bibliography
